Town of Spruce Pine v. Avery County


No. 431A96


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


346 N.C. 787; 488 S.E.2d 144; 1997 N.C. LEXIS 476


March 17, 1997, Heard in the Supreme Court  
July 24, 1997, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by additional defendants pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-30(2) from the decision of a divided panel of the Court of Appeals, 123 N.C. App. 704, 475 S.E.2d 233 (1996), reversing an order for summary judgment by Thornburg, J., entered on 23 June 1994 in Superior Court, Avery County. On 10 October 1996, this Court allowed discretionary review of an additional issue.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, town and utility company, brought an action against defendants, county and board of commissioners. The county challenged the constitutionality of the Water Supply Watershed Protection Act. The court of appeals (North Carolina) reversed the trial court's granting of the county's motion for summary judgment. Additional defendants, various state environmental agencies including the Environmental Management Commission (EMC), appealed.


OVERVIEW: The construction of a water intake by the town on a river in the county was going to make the river watershed subject to certain regulations of the EMC. The county contended that the WSWPA constituted an unconstitutional delegation of the power to legislate. The court held that the WSWPA was not an unconstitutional delegation of legislative power. N.C. Gen. Stat. § 143-214.5(b) (1996) specifically directed the management of watersheds by controlling development density, performance-based alternatives, or a combination of both. The procedural safeguards were adequate. The rule-making power of the EMC was subject to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 143-214.1(e) (1996). The General Assembly created the Watershed Protection Advisory Council consisting of representatives of state and local government as well as special interest groups which met with the EMC seven times before rules were adopted. The WSWPA required the EMC to submit reports quarterly to the Environmental Review Commission. In adopting rules, 40 informational meetings and eight public hearings were held across the state. The failure of an amendment to the WSWPA to pass constitutional muster did not condemn the entire Act.


OUTCOME: The court reversed summary judgment in favor of the EMC.

State v. Cummings


No. 4A95


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


346 N.C. 291; 488 S.E.2d 550; 1997 N.C. LEXIS 480


September 11, 1996, Heard in the Supreme Court  
July 24, 1997, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied January 26, 1998, Reported at: 1998 U.S. LEXIS 713.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Thompson, J., on 16 December 1994 in Superior Court, Brunswick County, upon a jury verdict of guilty of first-degree murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to additional judgments imposed for robbery with a dangerous weapon and assault with a deadly weapon with intent to kill was allowed 19 January 1996.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the trial court, which convicted him of murder and sentenced him to death. His appeal was automatic pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a).


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first-degree murder. He contended that the trial court erred in dismissing prospective jurors after conducting private unrecorded bench discussions with the prospective jurors. The court held that any error was harmless and that appellant's statements to the police were voluntary; thus, the trial court properly denied his motion to suppress his confession. The court also held that considering the evidence and the prosecutor's entire argument to the jury, the prosecutor's jury argument was proper and the trial court did not err in failing to intervene ex mero motu. Finally, the court stated that because appellant had badly beaten a defenseless, elderly woman in her own home and left her to die, the trial court's sentence of death was not disproportionate and was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment convicting appellant of murder and the sentence of death were affirmed. Any error was harmless and appellant's statements to the police were voluntary. The trial court properly denied his motion to suppress his confession. The prosecutor's jury argument was proper and the trial court did not err in failing to intervene ex mero motu. The trial court's sentence of death was not disproportionate and was affirmed.

State v. Allen


No. 115A96


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


346 N.C. 731; 488 S.E.2d 188; 1997 N.C. LEXIS 483


May 12, 1997, Heard in the Supreme Court  
July 24, 1997, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of life imprisonment entered by Downs, J., on 12 October 1995 in Superior Court, Buncombe County, upon a jury verdict of guilty of first-degree murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to additional judgments for attempted first-degree murder, assault with a deadly weapon with intent to kill, and discharging a firearm into occupied property was allowed 27 November 1996.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Superior Court, Buncombe County (North Carolina), of first degree murder, attempted first degree murder, assault with a deadly weapon with intent to kill, and discharging a firearm into occupied property and was sentenced to life imprisonment without parole.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant argued the sentence of life imprisonment without parole violated the Separation of Powers Clause, the constitutional prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment, and was not recognized by the North Carolina Constitution, and the trial court erred in admitting evidence of a prior assault on the murder victim and in instructing the jury on the issue of flight. The appellate court held that: (1) defendant's sentence did not violate the Separation of Powers Clause where the governor no longer possessed the constitutional power to parole; (2) the term "life imprisonment without parole" fell within the meaning of the constitutional term "imprisonment"; (3) defendant's sentence did not violate the constitutional prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment where it did not restrict the governor's clemency powers and was not otherwise unusual; (4) defendant's prior assault on the victim, his wife, was relevant to show his intent to kill her and was admissible under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 8C-1, N.C. R. Evid. 404(b) (1992); and (5) the trial court properly instructed the jury on the issue of flight, where there was evidence defendant had fled the crime scene.


OUTCOME: The appellate court concluded that defendant received a fair trial, free of prejudicial error, and affirmed the conviction and sentence.

DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court, Nash County (North Carolina), which convicted defendant for first-degree murder, felony murder, lying in wait, and first-degree kidnapping, and was sentenced to death for the first-degree murder conviction.


OVERVIEW: A jury had convicted defendant for first-degree murder, felony murder, lying in wait, and first-degree kidnapping, and sentenced him to death for the first-degree murder conviction. The trial court had properly excused six prospective jurors for cause, and properly refused to allow defendant to question them. Also, there was sufficient evidence supporting defendant's convictions of first-degree murder, felony murder, lying in wait, and first-degree kidnapping. Finally, there was nothing in the record that suggested defendant's death sentence was imposed under the influence of passion, prejudice, or any other arbitrary factor, and a review of similar cases showed the sentence was not disproportionate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, which convicted defendant for first-degree murder, felony murder, lying in wait, and first degree kidnapping, because defendant received a fair trial free from any prejudicial errors. The court affirmed defendant's sentence of death because was not disproportionate.

State v. Tyler


No. 11A96


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


346 N.C. 187; 485 S.E.2d 599; 1997 N.C. LEXIS 304


April 16, 1997, Heard in the Supreme Court  
June 6, 1997, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied December 1, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 7168.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Grant (Cy A.), J., at the 16 October 1995 Special Criminal Session of Superior Court, Hertford County.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review from a judgment of the Superior Court, Hertford County (North Carolina), which convicted defendant of first-degree murder and imposed a sentence of death in a case brought by plaintiff, the State of North Carolina.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of pouring gasoline on his girlfriend and setting her on fire with a match. Defendant argued that the trial court erred in admitting the testimony of a nurse regarding out-of-court statements made by the victim before her death in which she identified defendant. The court held that the trial court did not err in holding that the victim's statements possessed circumstantial guarantees of trustworthiness. There was no indication that the victim had any reason not to tell the truth after learning that defendant was in jail and could no longer hurt her. However, the trial court erred in relying upon corroborating evidence in reaching the conclusion that the statements were trustworthy. The evidence fully supported the conclusion of law. Therefore, the error was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt. Further, the record supported the sole aggravating circumstance found by the jury that the murder was heinous, atrocious, or cruel. The court concluded that the case had the characteristics of first-degree murders for which it had previously upheld the death penalty as proportionate. Accordingly, the death sentence was not excessive or disproportionate.


OUTCOME: The court upheld defendant's conviction of first-degree murder and the sentence of death.

State v. Thomas

SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


346 N.C. 135; 484 S.E.2d 368; 1997 N.C. LEXIS 210

41 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Price v. Howard


No. 312A96


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


346 N.C. 68; 484 S.E.2d 528; 1997 N.C. LEXIS 207


November 13, 1996, Heard in the Supreme Court  
May 9, 1997, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by plaintiff pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-30(2) from the decision of a divided panel of the Court of Appeals, 122 N.C. App. 674, 471 S.E.2d 673 (1996), affirming in part and reversing in part a custody order entered by Chaney, J., on 29 March 1995 in District Court, Durham County.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff caretaker filed suit against defendant natural mother for legal custody of a child who lived with the caretaker. The District Court, Durham County (North Carolina), concluded that it was in the child's best interest that custody be awarded to the caretaker, but awarded custody to the mother because she was a natural parent; the Court of Appeals affirmed the decision. The caretaker sought review.


OVERVIEW: The chief issue was what circumstances could require the constitutionally-protected interest of natural parenthood to yield to the "best interest of the child" test prescribed by N.C. Gen. Stat. § 50-13.2(a). The court held that conduct inconsistent with the parents' protected status, which need not rise to the statutory level warranting termination of parental rights, could result in application of the "best interest of the child" test without offending the Due Process Clause. Where the responsibility of a parent to act in the best interest of his or her child required temporary relinquishment of custody, the parent should notify the custodian upon relinquishment of custody that the relinquishment was temporary. Because the trial court had made no findings about whether the caretaker and the mother agreed that the surrender of custody would be temporary or about the degree of contact that the mother maintained with the child after the parties separated, the court could not conclude whether the mother should prevail based upon the constitutionally protected status of a natural parent or whether the "best interest of the child" test should be applied.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the lower appellate court and remanded the cause for a determination of whether the mother's conduct was inconsistent with the constitutionally protected status of a natural parent. If so, the trial court should determine custody based on the "best interest of the child" standard.

App. 256, 472 S.E.2d 605 (1996), dismissing plaintiffs' appeal from the order entered 9 June 1995 by Cashwell, J., in the Superior Court, Wake County, and remanding for dismissal of the amended complaint and petition for judicial review.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, an advocacy group and individuals, petitioned for discretionary review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (North Carolina) that reversed a judgment of the superior court affirming a rule promulgated by defendant North Carolina Commission for Health Services that suspended anonymous HIV testing by local health departments.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, an advocacy group and individuals, petitioned defendant North Carolina Commission on Health Services to rescind its newly promulgated rule that suspended anonymous HIV testing by local health boards. Defendant denied plaintiffs' rule petition and plaintiffs filed a petition for judicial review in the superior court. The superior court affirmed the validity of the rule and plaintiffs appealed. The appellate court reversed and held that the courts were without jurisdiction to review a rule promulgated by a state agency and remanded the action to the trial court with instructions to dismiss plaintiffs' petition. On plaintiffs' petition for discretionary review, the court reversed and held that defendant's rule was subject to judicial review under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 150B-20(d), that defendant's rule was supported by substantial evidence, and that defendant's denial of plaintiffs' rule petition was not arbitrary and capricious. OUTCOME: On a petition for discretionary review filed by plaintiffs, an advocacy group and individuals, the court reversed a judgment reversing a superior court judgment that affirmed a rule promulgated by defendant North Carolina Commission for Health Services that suspended anonymous HIV testing by local health departments. The court held that defendant's denial of a petition to rescind the rule was subject to judicial review.

Faulkenbury v. Teachers' & State Emples. Retirement Sys.

DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state officials challenged a judgment of the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina), which found in favor of plaintiffs, state and local employees, on their claim that a legislative reduction in pension benefits was an impermissible impairment of contract under U.S. Const. art. I, § 10.


OVERVIEW: State and local government employees objected to a statutory reduction in the retirement and disability benefits to which they were entitled due to vesting. The court on appeal agreed that such a reduction was unconstitutional because the employees had bargained for such benefits when they became employed and a contract was created. The court held that the contract could not be impaired merely because the General Assembly found that a new calculation would be more equitable or more beneficial to the state. The court further found that this did not bargain away a necessary state power, that N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 128-27(i) and 135-5(n) allowed the employees three years from any continuing reduced payment in which to bring suit, that the employees were entitled to the reductions taken from them as well as to interest on those funds, and that the employees were entitled to their attorneys' fees out of the litigation fund created.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of the state and local employees, and remanded the case for further proceedings.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court, Forsyth County (North Carolina) convicted defendant of first-degree murder and robbery with a firearm. The trial court sentenced him to death and 40 years imprisonment. Defendant appealed, alleging trial court error in pre-selection jury instruction, in jury selection, in denial of motion to dismiss, in admitting prejudicial evidence, in allowing unfair cross-examination and improper prosecutorial comment, and in sentencing.


OVERVIEW: The murder victim was an off-duty police officer who was waiting in line at a register when the robbery occurred. He then chased defendant, a struggle ensued and defendant fatally shot him. At the sentencing proceeding, the state presented evidence that defendant had been convicted once for common law robbery and three times for armed robbery. The jury also found five nonstatutory mitigating circumstances and the catchall mitigating circumstance. The jury recommended a sentence of death. The court found that defendant received a fair trial, free from prejudicial error, and that the sentence of death entered in the case was to be left undisturbed.

State v. Moody


No. 64A96


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


345 N.C. 563; 481 S.E.2d 629; 1997 N.C. LEXIS 34


December 12, 1996, Heard in the Supreme Court  
March 7, 1997, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied October 6, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 5481. Petition denied by: Moody v. North Carolina, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 6420 (U.S. Oct. 7, 2002).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Greeson, J., at the 10 July 1995 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Davidson County.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction from the Superior Court (North Carolina). Defendant pleaded guilty to first-degree murder; then, the trial court conducted a capital sentencing proceeding under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000 in which the jury found aggravating and mitigating circumstances and recommended death. The trial court followed the recommendation.


OVERVIEW: Defendant shot and killed his lover's husband and was charged with first-degree murder. During trial, he changed his plea to guilty. The jury found as aggravating circumstances that he had previously been convicted of a violent felony and that this murder was committed for pecuniary circumstances, as well as finding six mitigating circumstances. Defendant was sentenced to death. The court affirmed the sentence. The court held that there was no error in not suppressing victim's letters. They were introduced to show that victim loved his wife, which rebutted one of defendant's theories of mitigation. The recital of a poem by the prosecutor during closing argument was not so grossly improper as to require the trial court to intervene. There was no error in the jury instructions or in refusing defendant's proposed instructions. There was no error in telling the jury defendant was sick during the trial, or denying a request for individual voir dire. The record supported a finding of aggravating circumstances, there was no influence or passion, prejudice, or other arbitrary consideration, and the death sentence was not disproportionate to the penalty imposed in similar cases.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's death sentence. The court held that there was no error in not suppressing letters from the victim showing how he loved his wife, the jury instructions were proper, the prosecutor's poem regarding death was not grossly improper, refusal of defendant's request for individual voir dire was within the trial court's discretion, and the punishment was not disproportionate.

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal as of right by defendant pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from judgments imposing two sentences of death entered by McHugh, J., at the 27 October 1995 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Surry County, upon two jury verdicts finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to additional judgments was allowed by this Court 19 March 1996.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The defendant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Surry County (North Carolina), which convicted him of two counts of first degree murder and other crimes associated with the murders. Defendant also challenged the death sentence imposed after his conviction.


OVERVIEW: The defendant was convicted of first degree murder, as well as other charges stemming from the incident, and he was sentenced to death. The defendant appealed the conviction and sentence on numerous grounds. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment convicting defendant and sentencing him to death. The court held that the defendant received a fair trial and that the capital sentencing proceeding was free of prejudicial error. Specifically, the court found that the trial court committed no error by ordering the defense expert to prepare a written report of his findings for the state. The court concluded that the trial court was authorized to do so under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-905(b). Moreover, the court indicated that no physician-patient privilege was created between a physician and a criminal defendant who was examined in order to determine whether the defendant was able to stand trial. The court also found that there was no abuse of discretion in admitting photographs of the crime scene into evidence. Finally, the court concluded that a witness' hearsay statement was admissible under the state of mind exception to the hearsay rule.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, holding that the defendant received a fair trial and that the capital sentencing was free of prejudicial error. The court found that, based on the nature of the crime and the defendant, as a matter of law that the sentence of death was neither excessive nor disproportionate.

OVERVIEW: The defendant murdered one victim during a robbery and then raped and murdered the other victim, who was the first victim's daughter. OUTCOME: The court affirmed the sentence of death imposed on defendant for the double murder of a mother and her daughter.

Fulton Corp. v. Faulkner


No. 305A93-2


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


345 N.C. 419; 481 S.E.2d 8; 1997 N.C. LEXIS 3


September 9, 1996, Heard in the Supreme Court  
February 10, 1997, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Fulton Corp. v. Faulkner, 345 N.C. 761, 485 S.E.2d 47, 1997 N.C. LEXIS 281 (1997)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On remand from the Supreme Court of the United States.
Fulton Corp. v. Justus, 110 N.C. App. 493, 430 S.E.2d 494, 1993 N.C. App. LEXIS 561 (1993)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART, REVERSED IN PART, AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: This case was on remand from the United States Supreme Court and the court was required to determine the remedy to be applied after a portion of the intangibles tax statute had been declared unconstitutional. The appeals court had determined that the unconstitutional part of the statute should be severed and the remainder of the statute enforced.


OVERVIEW: After a portion of the intangibles tax statute was found to be unconstitutional the appeals court held that the part of the statute which was unconstitutional should be severed and the balance of the statute should be enforced. The court affirmed this decision entered by the appeals court because N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-215 (1992) clearly showed that the North Carolina General Assembly intended that if any part of the statute providing for an intangible tax was declared unconstitutional, that part should be severed for the statute, and the balance of the statute should be enforced. The court found that the offending portions were not so interconnected with the other parts that enforcement could not be completed. The court reversed the portion of the appeals court's decision that did not apply the rule of the case retroactively. Specifically, the court found that the United States Supreme Court required that its application of a rule of federal law be given retroactive effect.


OUTCOME: The appeals court's decision was affirmed because the court found that the unconstitutional portions of the intangibles tax statute and the other parts were not so interrelated or mutually dependent that the imposition of the tax could not be done without reference to the offending part. The court reversed the part of the decision which held that the rule should not be enforced retroactively.

In re Spivey


No. 36PA96


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


345 N.C. 404; 480 S.E.2d 693; 1997 N.C. LEXIS 18


September 13, 1996, Heard in the Supreme Court  
February 10, 1997, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On discretionary review, prior to determination by the Court of Appeals, pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-31, of an order entered by Allsbrook, J., on 29 August 1995 in Superior Court, New Hanover County, removing respondent Jerry L. Spivey from the Office of District Attorney for the Fifth Judicial District.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent district attorney challenged the order of the Superior Court of New Hanover County (North Carolina), which removed him from the Office of District Attorney pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-66, following notice and a hearing.


OVERVIEW: The uncontested evidence showed that the district attorney was at a bar in the early hours of a morning. While there, he loudly and repeatedly addressed a black patron, using a derogatory and abusive racial epithet. The district attorney was forcefully removed from the premises despite his unruly objections. The district attorney argued that the General Assembly was without constitutional authority to pass § 7A-66. He argued that he was an independent constitutional officer and the only possible method for his removal from office was by impeachment pursuant to N.C. Const. art. IV, §§ 1, 4. The court did not agree and affirmed the order to permanently remove the district attorney from office. The court held that the General Assembly had authority to pass § 7A-66. The court held that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 123-5 (1986) was an exclusive listing of officers liable for impeachment and the statute did not allow for impeachment of district attorneys. The court concluded that impeachment of district attorneys was not within the intent of either the state Constitution or the statute and that district attorneys were not subject to removal by impeachment.


OUTCOME: The order removing the district attorney from his office was affirmed.

Moore v. City of Creedmoor


No. 435A95


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


345 N.C. 356; 481 S.E.2d 14; 1997 N.C. LEXIS 15


September 12, 1996, Heard in the Supreme Court  
February 10, 1997, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendants pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-30(2) from the decision of a divided panel of the Court of Appeals, 120 N.C. App. 27, 460 S.E.2d 899 (1995), reversing in part and affirming in part an order entered by Ellis, J., on 25 May 1993 in Superior Court, Granville County. On 8 February 1996, this Court allowed plaintiffs' petition for discretionary review of additional issues pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-31.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART, REVERSED IN PART, AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, owners of a dinette patronized by the African-American community, brought suit for malicious prosecution and other claims against defendants, city and city officials, after a nuisance abatement action was filed against the dinette. The North Carolina Court of Appeals affirmed the entry of summary judgment against the dinette owners' constitutional and other claims but remanded the case for trial of the malicious prosecution claim.


OVERVIEW: The dinette owners sued the city and city officials for malicious prosecution, intentional infliction of emotional distress, and violation of federal and state constitutional rights. The lower appellate court affirmed the trial court's entry of summary judgment against the dinette owners' constitutional claims and claims of intentional infliction of emotional distress but reversed the entry of summary judgment on the malicious prosecution claim. Both parties appealed. In reversing in part and affirming in part, the court held that (1) where the city adopted a resolution to prosecute the dinette owners and an injunction forbidding the dinette's operation, there was an issue of fact whether the dinette owners' freedom of speech was thereby abridged and thus suit under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 was appropriate, (2) the city officials could be sued in their official capacities, (3) whether the city officials in their individual capacities retaliated against dinette owners because of their free speech exercise was a question of fact, (4) the city officials were not entitled to qualified immunity, and (5) there was an issue of genuine fact as to whether the city officials acted out of malice.


OUTCOME: The lower appellate court's judgment sustaining the trial court's grant of summary judgment on the dinette owners' constitutional claims and claims for punitive damages were reversed and the case remanded to the lower appellate court for further remand to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with the court's opinion.

Soles v. City of Raleigh Civ. Serv. Comm'n


No. 280PA95


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


345 N.C. 443; 480 S.E.2d 685; 1997 N.C. LEXIS 16; 12 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1304


February 13, 1996, Heard in the Supreme Court  
February 10, 1997, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On discretionary review pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-31 of a unanimous decision of the Court of Appeals, 119 N.C. App. 88, 457 S.E.2d 746 (1995), affirming judgment holding unconstitutional a provision in Raleigh Civil Service Act Rule. 0504 entered by Allen (W. Steven, Sr.), J., at the 6 July 1992 Civil Session of Superior Court, Wake County.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged a judgment from the Court of Appeals (North Carolina), which affirmed the trial court's order reversing the city's termination of appellee employee for detrimental personal conduct. The lower courts held that Raleigh, N.C., Civil Service Commission R. .0504 violated the employee's constitutional right to due process.


OVERVIEW: The employee was discharged by the city for detrimental personal conduct. The employee challenged the administrative decision on the ground that the burden of proof set forth in Raleigh, N.C., Civil Service Commission R. .0504 violated his constitutional right to procedural due process. The trial court reversed the termination, and the appellate court affirmed. On further review, the court reversed and held that: (1) in the absence of a contractual agreement between the city and the employee that established a definite period of employment, the employment was presumed to be an "at-will" employment, terminable at the will of either party; (2) an employer's personnel manual or policies were not part of an employee's contract of employment unless they were expressly included in the contract; (3) the employee had no property right in his employment and was not entitled to due process.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment that affirmed the determination that the employee's termination by the city was unconstitutional. The court remanded the cause for further proceedings.

State v. Westbrooks


No. 428A94


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


345 N.C. 43; 478 S.E.2d 483; 1996 N.C. LEXIS 648


November 14, 1995, Heard in the Supreme Court  
December 6, 1996, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of life imprisonment entered by Cornelius, J., at the 1 November 1993 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Guilford County, upon a jury verdict of guilty of first-degree murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to additional judgments for conspiracy to commit murder and solicitation to commit murder was allowed 27 January 1995.



DISPOSITION: NO. 92CRS43698, FIRST-DEGREE MURDER: NO ERROR. NO. 92CRS20473, COUNT 1, CONSPIRACY TO COMMIT MURDER: NO ERROR. NO. 92CRS20473, COUNT 2, SOLICITATION TO COMMIT MURDER: JUDGMENT ARRESTED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Criminal Session of Superior Court Guilford County (North Carolina), which imposed a life sentence on defendant for first-degree murder on charges brought by the State. Defendant filed a motion to bypass the appellate court as to judgments for conspiracy to commit murder and solicitation to commit murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged and criminally convicted following the murder of her disabled husband. On appeal, defendant contended that the trial court used the same item of evidence to prove more than one aggravating factor in both the conspiracy and solicitation cases. The court disagreed because the language on the sentencing form was explanatory, not a separate factor. Thus, the trial court did not find two factors in aggravation based on the same evidence. The court agreed that solicitation to commit murder was a lesser offense of murder as an accessory before the fact. Under the definitional approach, the court vacated defendant's conviction for solicitation to commit murder. The indictment and the evidence supported the verdict of murder. Witnesses' statements regarding the victim's then existing state of mind were admissible. Further, given the overwhelming evidence against defendant, there was no reasonable possibility that, had the evidence not been admitted, a different result would have been reached. Defendant was not induced to remain silent before her arrest, and the use of her prearrest silence for impeachment did not violate her Fifth Amendment rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction for first-degree murder, affirmed the conviction for conspiracy to commit murder, and arrested the judgment for solicitation to commit murder.

State v. Stroud


No. 162A95


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


345 N.C. 106; 478 S.E.2d 476; 1996 N.C. LEXIS 653


May 15, 1996, Heard in the Supreme Court  
December 6, 1996, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied October 6, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 4830.
Writ of certiorari denied Stroud v. North Carolina, 522 U.S. 826, 118 S. Ct. 86, 139 L. Ed. 2d 43, 1997 U.S. LEXIS 4830 (1997)
Motion denied by State v. Stroud, 546 S.E.2d 392, 2000 N.C. LEXIS 1005 (N.C., 2000)
Motion denied by State v. Stroud, 546 S.E.2d 392, 2000 N.C. LEXIS 985 (N.C., 2000)
Petition denied by State v. Stroud, 353 N.C. 531, 550 S.E.2d 163, 2001 N.C. LEXIS 629 (2001)
Motion dismissed by State v. Stroud, 353 N.C. 524, 550 S.E.2d 165, 2001 N.C. LEXIS 627 (2001)
Petition denied by State v. Stroud, 360 N.C. 581, 636 S.E.2d 197, 2006 N.C. LEXIS 855 (2006)
Writ of certiorari dismissed State v. Stroud, 779 S.E.2d 145, 2015 N.C. LEXIS 1200 (N.C., 2015)
Motion dismissed by State v. Stroud, 2016 N.C. LEXIS 84 (N.C., Jan. 28, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal as of right by defendant pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Allen (J.B., Jr.), J., at the 23 January 1995 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Durham County, upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to an additional judgment imposed for second-degree kidnapping was allowed 30 November 1995.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed as of right from a judgment of the Criminal Session of the Superior Court, Durham County (North Carolina), which imposed the death sentence upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of felony murder by torture and of second-degree kidnapping.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that all three theories of first-degree murder submitted to the jury were tainted by the trial court's failure to dismiss the second-degree kidnapping charge. The court found no prejudicial error and held that: (1) the evidence was sufficient to establish that the blows used for restraining the victim were separate from the blows causing death; (2) the trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion to dismiss the second-degree kidnapping charge; (3) the trial court did not subject defendant to multiple punishment by submitting to the jury the second-degree kidnapping charge and the charge of felony murder based on the underlying felony of kidnapping; and (4) the death sentence was not imposed under the influence of passion, prejudice, or any other arbitrary factor and was proportional to the crime.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that the death sentence was not excessive or disproportionate and that defendant received a fair trial and capital sentencing proceeding free of prejudicial error.

State v. Norwood


No. 318A94


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


344 N.C. 511; 476 S.E.2d 349; 1996 N.C. LEXIS 497


December 13, 1995, Heard In The Supreme Court  
October 11, 1996, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Sumner, J., at the 16 May 1994 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Nash County, upon a verdict of guilty of first-degree murder. The defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals on his appeal from a conviction for willfully burning a building was allowed by this Court on 27 April 1995.



DISPOSITION: The defendant received a trial and capital sentencing proceeding free from prejudicial error, and the jury did not sentence the defendant out of prejudice or passion. The sentence is proportionate.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the conviction and sentence of the Superior Court, Nash County (North Carolina) for first-degree murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals on his appeal from a conviction for willfully burning a building was allowed.


OVERVIEW: The court denied defendant's claims alleging defects in the jury selection. The court held that the jury was properly death-qualified, that certain jurors were properly excused for cause because of their opposition to the death penalty, and that the challenges to certain jurors did not deprive him of his rights under Batson or the Sixth Amendment. The court held that defendant was not entitled to a petit jury composed in whole or in part of persons of a certain race. The court, with the aid of expert testimony, found that despite defendant's alleged mental retardation, defendant knew that burning someone was wrong. Therefore, the court upheld the constitutionality of the death penalty as applied to defendant. The court held that defendant failed to show that admission of the victim's pictures was unduly prejudicial or improper. The court held that there was sufficient evidence for a jury to find premeditation and deliberation in defendant's actions. The court found no error in the sentencing phase where defendant failed to show any resulting prejudice. The sentence was proportionate, supported by evidence, and not made disproportionate due to defendant's mental status.


OUTCOME: The court held that defendant received a trial and capital sentencing proceeding free from prejudicial error, that the jury did not sentence defendant out of prejudice or passion, and that the sentence was proportionate.

State v. Ocasio

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from two judgments imposing sentences of life imprisonment entered by Strickland, J., at the 16 January 1995 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Onslow County, upon jury verdicts of guilty of first-degree murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to additional judgments was allowed 12 February 1996.



DISPOSITION: In No. 92CRS13020 -- First-Degree Murder -- NO ERROR. No. 92CRS13021 -- First-Degree Murder -- NO ERROR. No. 92CRS13022 -- Second-Degree Burglary and Larceny -- NO ERROR. No. 92CRS13023 -- Kidnapping -- JUDGMENT ARRESTED. No. 92CRS13024 -- Kidnapping -- JUDGMENT ARRESTED. No. 92CRS11964 -- Larceny of Motor Vehicle -- NO ERROR. No. 92CRS11965 -- Larceny of Motor Vehicle -- NO ERROR. No. 92CRS11966 -- Breaking and Entering and Larceny -- NO ERROR. No. 92CRS11967 -- Safecracking -- NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgments of the Superior Court, Onslow County (North Carolina), which convicted him of two counts of murder in the first degree, second-degree burglary and larceny, kidnapping, two counts of larceny of a motor vehicle, and for breaking and entering, larceny, and safecracking.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought review of the trial court's judgments entered upon jury verdicts for conviction on two counts of murder in the first degree, second-degree burglary and larceny, kidnapping, two counts of larceny of a motor vehicle, and for breaking and entering, larceny, and safecracking. The court arrested judgments for kidnapping which served as the underlying offense for felony murder merged with the higher level offenses. However, the court affirmed the judgments as to all of defendant's other convictions and sentencing because there were no evidentiary, discovery or sentencing errors, finding that the trial court acted with in its discretion as to each of the points on these matters disputed by defendant.


OUTCOME: The court arrested the trial court's judgments as to defendant's kidnapping convictions because kidnapping was the underlying offense for defendant's felony murder convictions and merged with those offenses. However, the court affirmed the judgments as to all of defendant's other convictions and sentencing because there were no evidentiary, discovery or sentencing errors.

State v. Wilkinson


No. 465A94


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


344 N.C. 198; 474 S.E.2d 375; 1996 N.C. LEXIS 490


April 10, 1996, Heard In The Supreme Court  
September 6, 1996, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected October 22, 1996.
Writ denied by State v. Wilkinson, 353 N.C. 279, 546 S.E.2d 394, 2000 N.C. LEXIS 1108 (2000)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Wilkinson v. Polk, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 7926 (4th Cir. N.C., Apr. 5, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from judgments imposing sentences of death entered by Johnson (E. Lynn), J., at the 22 August 1994 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Cumberland County, upon pleas of guilty for three counts of first-degree murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to additional judgments imposed was allowed on 7 August 1995.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed death sentences entered by the Superior Court, Cumberland County (North Carolina) after defendant pled guilty to three counts of first-degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was sentenced to death after he pled guilty to three counts of murder. On appeal, the court affirmed the death sentences. The court held that absent an absolute impasse between defendant and his counsel, decisions regarding tactical decisions were within the province of counsel after consultation with defendant. The court refused to review objections to several instructions given at the request of defendant on the grounds of invited error. Defendant could be found guilty of sexual assault even if the assaults occurred after the victims died because the assaults were a part of a continuous transaction that began when the victims were alive. There was sufficient evidence that defendant committed two of the murders based upon his desire to avoid arrest. The submission of similar aggravating circumstances was not redundant because identical evidence was not required to establish the two aggravating circumstances. The trial court did not abuse its discretion when it excused three jurors who stated that they would be unable to recommend a death sentence. Defendant's death sentences were not disproportionate compared to similar cases in which the death penalty was imposed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the sentences of death entered against defendant because defendant received a fair capital sentencing proceeding that was free from prejudicial error, and considering both the crime and defendant, the death sentences were not disproportionate or excessive compared to similar cases in which the death penalty was imposed.

State v. Elliott


No. 224A94

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Long (James M.), J., on 3 May 1994 in Superior Court, Davidson County, upon a jury verdict of guilty of first-degree murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to an additional judgment imposed for felony child abuse was allowed on 17 April 1995.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed as of right pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment entered in the Superior Court of Davidson County (North Carolina), which imposed a sentence of death upon conviction for first-degree murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to an additional judgment imposed for felony child abuse was allowed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was tried capitally for the first-degree murder of the two-year old daughter of his girlfriend, whom defendant was entrusted to care for while the mother worked. The evidence revealed that the child had numerous injuries and died from a severe head injury caused by defendant's repeatedly "slamming" her head on the floor. The jury returned a verdict finding defendant guilty of murder as based on premeditation and deliberation and recommended a sentence of death. The court held that the jury selection, guilt-innocence phase, and sentencing proceeding of defendant's trial were free from prejudicial error and that the death sentence was not disproportionate in light of the evidence. The court found that the jury could have concluded that defendant knew that his conduct would result in the child's death and that he intended to kill her. The court also held that statements made by the prosecutor during the penalty phase, including suggestions as to what the victim may have been thinking as she was dying, were proper as based upon the evidence presented at trial and reasonable inferences from the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for first-degree murder and felony child abuse, and the sentence of death.

State v. Womble


No. 126A94 - Columbus


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


343 N.C. 667; 473 S.E.2d 291; 1996 N.C. LEXIS 408


September 13, 1995, Heard In The Supreme Court  
July 31, 1996, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied January 21, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 588.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal as of right by defendant pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Gore, J., at the 14 February 1994 Special Criminal Session of Superior Court, Columbus County.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a), defendant appealed from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by the Special Criminal Session of Superior Court, Columbus County (North Carolina).


OVERVIEW: Following a capital sentencing proceeding pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000, the jury recommended that defendant be sentenced to death. Defendant appealed, and the court concluded that the jury selection and defendant's capital sentencing proceeding were free from prejudicial error, and that the sentence of death was not disproportionate. Defendant claimed that the trial court erred by permitting the state to reexamine a prospective juror whom it had previously passed. The court held that a "good reason" existed for reopening the examination of the prospective juror and the trial court did not abuse its discretion. The court held that with respect to a tape recording admitted into evidence, portions of the tape were inaudible but there were other parts that were very clear and extremely intelligible and audible to the court and that it was relevant and admissible. The court held that, based on the nature of defendant's crime, the court could not conclude as a matter of law that the sentence of death was excessive or disproportionate. The court held that defendant received a fair sentencing proceeding, free of prejudicial error.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. The court concluded that the jury selection and defendant's capital sentencing proceeding were free from prejudicial error, and that the sentence of death was not disproportionate.

State v. Bishop


No. 207A94 - Guilford


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


343 N.C. 518; 472 S.E.2d 842; 1996 N.C. LEXIS 412


November 15, 1995, Heard In The Supreme Court  
July 31, 1996, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied January 21, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 616.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Rousseau, J., on 27 April 1994 in Superior Court, Guilford County, upon a jury verdict of guilty of first-degree murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to additional judgments and sentences was allowed 25 April 1995.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendant appealed his death sentence under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a) by the Superior Court, Guilford County (North Carolina) following a jury verdict that he was guilty of first degree murder. Defendant was also convicted of breaking and entering, robbery with a dangerous weapon, first-degree kidnapping, financial transaction card fraud, and conspiracy, and was deemed a habitual felon.


OVERVIEW: Criminal defendant was convicted of first degree murder and several other crimes and sentenced to death. The court affirmed the lower court's sentence, finding that there had not been abuse of discretion or prejudice when the lower court refused to permit inquiry as to jurors' feelings regarding the death penalty. Similarly, the court found that the jury instructions issued concerning the meaning of a life sentence had been proper because a defendant's eligibility for parole was not a matter for jury consideration. The court found that opinion testimony from lay witnesses was properly admitted as having been rationally based on witness perceptions and helpful to understanding the issues. The court held defendant failed to demonstrate that disputed prosecutorial arguments caused gross impropriety. The court noted evidence of a codefendant's sentence was properly deemed not relevant to the jury's sentencing determination and that evidence of defendant's past violent felonies was properly admitted at sentencing. Finally, the court found that defendant's sentence was proportional to the crime of witness killing and upheld the forfeiture of his truck under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 90-112.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, finding that there had been neither abuse of discretion nor prejudice at voir dire. The court held that jury instructions concerning the meaning of a life sentence had been proper, that opinion testimony from lay witnesses had been properly admitted, that the prosecutor's closing arguments and the jury instructions were made properly, and that sentencing had been fair and the sentence proportional.

State v. Penland


No. 139A94 - Stokes


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


343 N.C. 634; 472 S.E.2d 734; 1996 N.C. LEXIS 397


February 12, 1996, Heard In The Supreme Court  
July 31, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied January 21, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 634.
Writ of certiorari denied Penland v. North Carolina, 519 U.S. 1098, 117 S. Ct. 781, 136 L. Ed. 2d 725, 1997 U.S. LEXIS 634 (1997)
Writ dismissed by State v. Penland, 2006 N.C. LEXIS 424 (N.C., May 4, 2006)
Petition dismissed by State v. Penland, 2006 N.C. LEXIS 447 (N.C., May 4, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Wood, J., at the 24 January 1994 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Stokes County, upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to additional judgments imposed for first-degree rape, first-degree sexual offense, and first-degree kidnapping was allowed 9 February 1996.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the Superior Court, Stokes County (North Carolina), which convicted him of first degree murder, first-degree rape, first-degree sexual offense, and first-degree kidnapping. Defendant contended that the evidence was insufficient to support his death sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and his nephews picked up a prostitute. When she asked for money, defendant assaulted her, had her put on handcuffs, took her to the woods, sexually assaulted her, had his nephews tie her to a tree, and stabbed her. Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder, sexual offense, and rape and was sentenced to death. The court affirmed defendant's convictions and sentence. The court held that the deceased could withdraw consent anytime prior to penetration and that defendant's actions were constructive force for the purpose of the sexual assaults. His nephews' presence was enough to establish that defendant was aided and abetted because defendant was likely to regard their presence as encouragement. Testimony of defendant's prior bad acts was admissible because it demonstrated his modus operandi. The trial court was not required to give defendant's requested instruction verbatim as long as it gave it in substance. Defendant failed to produce the substantial evidence required to get mitigating circumstances submitted. The court held that defendant's death sentence was not excessive or disproportionate because it was premeditated, deliberate, and involved torture.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for first degree murder, rape, sexual offense, and kidnapping and his death sentence because the state could prove constructive force for the sexual assaults, there was evidence that defendant was aided and abetted in the crimes, the prior bad acts testimony was offered to prove a common plan or scheme, and defendant did not produce substantial evidence of the existence of mitigating circumstances.

No. 37A86-3 - Buncombe

Related proceeding at State v. Fullwood, 349 N.C. 234, 558 S.E.2d 188, 1998 N.C. LEXIS 699 (1998)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Remanded by Fullwood v. Lee, 290 F.3d 663, 2002 U.S. App. LEXIS 9579 (4th Cir. N.C., 2002)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Sitton, J., at the 1 August 1994 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Buncombe County.
State v. Fullwood, 329 N.C. 233, 404 S.E.2d 842, 1991 N.C. LEXIS 410 (1991)



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court, Buncombe County (North Carolina) convicted defendant of felonious breaking and entering and of first-degree murder and sentenced him to death for the murder conviction. Defendant appealed his death sentence as of right.


OVERVIEW: During voir dire, the trial court held that only one of defendant's two appointed attorneys was permitted to question prospective jurors. The jury found defendant guilty of first-degree murder under the theory of malice, premeditation, and deliberation. The only aggravating circumstance submitted to the jury was that the murder was especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(e)(9). The jury found this aggravating circumstance to exist. Jurors also found one or more mitigating circumstances to exist. The court held that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 84-14 (1985) was not violated because the trial court did not deny defendant the assistance of a second attorney or so drastically circumscribe the second attorney's role as to render the appointment of two attorneys meaningless. The court further concluded that the record fully supported the aggravating circumstance found by the jury and found that there was no indication that the death sentence was imposed under the influence of passion, prejudice, or any other arbitrary consideration. The court also held that the case was similar to six other cases in which the court found the death sentence proportionate.


OUTCOME: The court found no error in defendant's death sentence.

DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed as of right, pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a), from a judgment imposing two sentences of death entered by the Criminal Session of Superior Court, Moore County (North Carolina), on a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of two counts of first-degree murder. Defendant pled guilty to the first-degree murders of his stepmother and a security guard.

State v. Boyd


No. 547A88-2 - Rockingham


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


343 N.C. 699; 473 S.E.2d 327; 1996 N.C. LEXIS 414


February 16, 1996, Heard in the Supreme Court  
July 31, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied Boyd v. North Carolina, 519 U.S. 1096, 117 S. Ct. 778, 136 L. Ed. 2d 722, 1997 U.S. LEXIS 608 (1997)
Writ denied by State v. Boyd, 352 N.C. 150, 544 S.E.2d 229, 2000 N.C. LEXIS 511 (2000)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at, Application denied by, Motion denied by Boyd v. Lee, 2003 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 20893 (M.D.N.C., July 23, 2003)
Stay denied by State v. Boyd, 2005 N.C. LEXIS 1245 (N.C., Nov. 29, 2005)
Writ denied by State v. Boyd, 2005 N.C. LEXIS 1246 (N.C., Nov. 29, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from judgments imposing two sentences of death entered by Greeson, J., on 14 July 1994, in Superior Court, Rockingham County, upon two jury verdicts finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder.
State v. Boyd, 332 N.C. 101, 418 S.E.2d 471, 1992 N.C. LEXIS 362 (1992)



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Superior Court, Rockingham County (North Carolina), which imposed two sentences of death upon two jury verdicts finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder. Defendant argued that the trial court erred in prohibiting a doctor from testifying that defendant was not acting with a "cool state of mind" during the murders and in refusing to instruct the jury on the defense of intoxication and automatism.


OVERVIEW: The defendant shot and killed his estranged wife and his father-in-law, then reloaded and told his brother-in-law he was going to kill him next. The court held that the trial court did not err in: (1) refusing to allow a doctor to testify that the defendant was not in a "cool state of mind," as such testimony embraced precise legal terms, definitions of which were not readily apparent to medical experts and might be confusing to the jury; (2) allowing the brother-in-law to testify that he told his wife that the defendant was going to kill everybody, because it fell within the realm of lay testimony permitted by N.C. Gen. Stat. § 8C-1, N.C. R. Evid. 701 (1992); (3) not instructing the jury on defendant's intoxication, unconsciousness, or automatism at the time of the murders, because defendant gave a detailed confession to police within hours of the murders; and (4) in submitting that one killing was an aggravating circumstance for another murder for purposes of sentencing under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(e)(11). The court also concluded that the § 15A-2000(e)(11) aggravating circumstance of a course of conduct did not violate due process by reason of unconstitutional vagueness.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the superior court, which imposed two sentences of death upon two jury verdicts finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder.

State v. White


No. 94A94 - Mecklenburg


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


343 N.C. 378; 471 S.E.2d 593; 1996 N.C. LEXIS 326


February 13, 1996, Heard In The Supreme Court  
June 13, 1996, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected August 19, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Saunders, J., on 4 February 1994 in Superior Court, Mecklenburg County, upon a jury verdict of guilty of first-degree murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to other convictions and sentences was allowed 30 June 1995.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction after a jury trial in the Superior Court, Mecklenburg County, (North Carolina) on charges of first-degree murder, first-degree kidnapping, larceny of an automobile, robbery with a dangerous weapon, and second-degree burglary. The trial court sentenced defendant to death on the murder charge.


OVERVIEW: Defendant broke through the window of a girlfriend's home, killed her roommate, stole the victim's property, and fled in her automobile. Although defendant admitted that he killed the victim, he contended that he did not intend to kill her and that the killing occurred while he was under the influence of alcohol and cocaine. Defendant was convicted on charges of first-degree murder, first-degree kidnapping, larceny of an automobile, robbery with a dangerous weapon, and second-degree burglary. The trial court sentenced defendant to death on the murder charge. On appeal, the court affirmed the convictions and sentence. The court held that: (1) the trial court did not abuse its discretion in excusing for cause a prospective juror who answered voir dire questions by expressing doubts about the death penalty; (2) the jury was properly instructed that defendant's eligibility for parole was not a matter for consideration; (3) evidence of defendant's prior assaults was admissible under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 8C-1, N.C. R. Evid. 404(b) (1995); (4) the jury was properly instructed concerning evidence of voluntary intoxication; and (5) the death sentence was not disproportionate to the crime.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence to death on charges of first-degree murder and on related felony charges. The court held that the trial court did not err in its evidentiary rulings or instructions to the jury, that a prospective juror who answered voir dire questions by expressing doubts about the death penalty was properly excused, and that the death sentence was not disproportionate to defendant's crime.

 

66 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. DeCastro


No. 221A93 - Johnston


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


342 N.C. 667; 467 S.E.2d 653; 1996 N.C. LEXIS 146


October 12, 1994, Heard In The Supreme Court  
March 8, 1996, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from two judgments imposing sentences of death entered by Stephens (Donald W.), J., at the 12 April 1993 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Johnston County. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to an additional judgment imposed for robbery with a dangerous weapon was allowed on 15 March 1994.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction for first degree murder and a death sentence in the Superior Court of Johnston County (North Carolina). Defendant argued that the trial court erred by failing to submit jury instructions regarding statutory mitigating circumstances, that it erred in instructing the jury that it could determine if defendant's age was a mitigating factor, and that the jury was improperly instructed on mitigation.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder and sentenced to death. Defendant sought review of the convictions and the death sentence, arguing that the trial court improperly admitted a knife found some time after the murders, that the prosecutor made improper comments during closing, that inadmissible character evidence was admitted, that the trial erred by failing to submit jury instructions, pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(f)(6), regarding the statutory mitigating circumstance that defendant's capacity to appreciate the criminality of his conduct was impaired. Defendant also argued that the trial court erred in instructing the jury that it could determine if defendant's age had chronological value as a mitigating circumstance. The court affirmed, stating that the trial court was required to submit, pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(f)(6) (1995), any statutory mitigating circumstance supported by the evidence. However, the consumption of alcohol by defendant was not enough to warrant submission of a statutory mitigating circumstance under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(f)(6). The court also held that defendant's age was not determinative of a mitigating circumstance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for murder and his death sentence, holding that although the trial court was required to submit any statutory mitigating circumstance supported by the evidence, the mere consumption of alcohol by defendant was not enough to warrant submission of a statutory mitigating circumstance. The court further held that defendant's chronological age was not a determinative mitigating circumstance.

State v. Chandler


No. 343A93 - Surry


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


342 N.C. 742; 467 S.E.2d 636; 1996 N.C. LEXIS 148


January 10, 1995, Heard In The Supreme Court  
March 8, 1996, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Freeman, J. on 20 July 1993 in Superior Court, Surry County, upon a jury verdict of guilty of first-degree murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to his appeal of his convictions of first-degree burglary and attempted larceny was allowed on 5 April 1994.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction from the Superior Court of Surry County (North Carolina) for first-degree murder under the felony murder rule, with first-degree burglary as the underlying felony, and for attempted larceny.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for fist-degree murder under the felony murder rule, with first-degree burglary as the underlying felony, and for attempted larceny. The jury recommended and the trial court imposed a sentence of death for the first-degree murder conviction and plus a prison term for the attempted larceny. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment of conviction and concluded that an erroneous inclusion of the word attempted in the original burglary instruction was not plain error. Defendant testified that he intended to steal marijuana when he broke into and entered the victim's home; thus, there was direct evidence presented at trial of defendant's intent to commit larceny sufficient to support the burglary conviction. The court concluded that the pecuniary gain aggravating circumstance was properly submitted to the jury because pecuniary gain was not an essential element of the crime of felony murder. The court found the evidence did not support defendant's contention that he was under a mental or emotional disturbance at the time of the murder and concluded the sentence of death recommended by the jury and imposed by the trial court was not disproportionate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment against defendant for first-degree murder under the felony murder rule, with first-degree burglary as the underlying felony, and for attempted larceny. The court concluded that defendant had received a fair trial, free from prejudice and error and held the sentence of death recommended by the jury and ordered by the trial court was not disproportionate.

Maready v. City of Winston-Salem


No. 422PA95 - Forsyth


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


342 N.C. 708; 467 S.E.2d 615; 1996 N.C. LEXIS 150


February 16, 1996, Heard In The Supreme Court  
March 8, 1996, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected April 2, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: On discretionary review pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-31, prior to a determination by the Court of Appeals, of a judgment entered on 28 August 1995 by Rousseau, J., in Superior Court, Forsyth County, enjoining defendants from making incentive grants or otherwise committing public funds for economic incentive purposes pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 158-7.1.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART; REVERSED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff citizen filed suit challenging N.C. Gen. Stat. § 158-7.1. The Superior Court of Forsyth County (North Carolina) found that the statute was unconstitutional, enjoined the local governing bodies from making further incentive grants, denied the citizen's motion for a mandatory injunction to require recovery of incentive grants, and dismissed the claim that the local governing bodies violated the Open Meetings Law. All parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: The citizen claimed that § 158-7.1, under which the local governing bodies were authorized to make economic development incentive grants to private corporations, was unconstitutional because it violated the public purpose clause in N.C. Const. art. V, § 2(1), and because it was impermissibly vague, ambiguous, and without reasonably objective standards. The court held that § 158-7.1 furthered a public purpose because (1) it was within the appropriate scope of governmental involvement and reasonably related to communal needs and (2) it primarily benefitted the public and not a private party by creating a more stable local economy. The court also held that the meaning and intent of § 158-7.1 were sufficiently clear to pass constitutional muster. The court further found that there was no violation of the North Carolina Open Meetings Law because there was sufficient evidence to support the trial court's finding that each closed session held to discuss economic development expenditures was preceded by a motion and approving vote to go into closed session at an open and regularly scheduled public meeting and because no action was taken at the closed meetings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's rulings that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 158-7.1 violated the public purpose clause of the North Carolina Constitution and that the statute was impermissibly vague and ambiguous. The court affirmed the trial court's ruling that the local governing bodies did not violate the Open Meetings Law.

 

69 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. Lineberger


No. 533A94 - Lincoln


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


342 N.C. 599; 467 S.E.2d 24; 1996 N.C. LEXIS 5


September 12, 1995, Heard In The Supreme Court  
February 9, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected February 28, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of life imprisonment entered by Sitton, J., on 15 December 1993 in Superior Court, Lincoln County, upon a jury verdict of guilty of first-degree murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to an additional judgment imposed for assault with a deadly weapon with intent to kill was allowed 31 October 1994.



DISPOSITION: NEW TRIAL.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Lincoln County (North Carolina), which convicted him of first-degree murder and sentenced him to life imprisonment following a capital sentencing proceeding. He was also found guilty of an assault with a deadly weapon with intent to kill and sentenced to a consecutive term of 10 years in prison.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that the trial court erred in refusing to consider a proposed plea agreement in which he agreed to enter a plea of guilty to second-degree murder and assault with a deadly weapon with intent to kill. The trial court concluded that it did not have the authority to accept the plea because there was sufficient evidence to try the case capitally. Upon review, the court reversed. It found that the Constitution did not prohibit the use of absolute prosecutorial discretion in determining which cases to prosecute for first-degree murder so long as such discretionary decisions were not based on race, religion, or some other impermissible classification. The prosecutor could accept a plea of guilty for second-degree murder or a lesser offense until defendant was determined to be guilty of first-degree murder by either plea or verdict. Not until then was the trial court bound to conduct a capital sentencing proceeding unless there was no evidence to support a finding of aggravating circumstances. The trial court acted under a misapprehension of law in refusing to consider the plea agreement.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction and remanded the action for a new trial. The court instructed the prosecutor to renew the plea offer defendant had accepted and presented to the trial court.

State v. Best

The court allowed defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to his conviction for first-degree burglary, first-degree rape, and robbery with a dangerous weapon.

OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

State v. Jaynes


No. 194A92 - Polk


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


342 N.C. 249; 464 S.E.2d 448; 1995 N.C. LEXIS 673



December 8, 1995, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected February 16, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Ferrell, J., on 23 April 1992 in Superior Court, Polk County, upon a jury verdict of guilty of first-degree murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals on additional convictions for first-degree arson, first-degree burglary, robbery with a dangerous weapon, and two counts of larceny of an automobile was allowed 9 July 1993. The appeal was heard in the Supreme Court 7 December 1993. On 2 March 1994, defendant filed a motion for appropriate relief with the Supreme Court, which remanded the case on 11 May 1994 to the Superior Court, Polk County, for an evidentiary hearing. The Superior Court denied the motion for appropriate relief in an order entered by Llewellyn, J., on 12 June 1995.



DISPOSITION: No. 90CRS1096 - First-Degree Murder -- No error in the trial; death sentence vacated and case remanded for new capital sentencing proceeding. No. 90CRS1097, First-Degree Arson -- No error. No. 90CRS1118, First-Degree Burglary -- No error. No. 91CRS466, Robbery with a dangerous weapon -- No error. No. 91CRS467, Felonious larceny -- Judgment arrested. No. 91CRS468, Felonious larceny [***2] -- Judgment arrested.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction from the Criminal Session of Superior Court, Polk County (North Carolina), for first degree murder, first degree arson, first degree burglary, robbery with a dangerous weapon, and felonious larceny of an automobile, and the judgment of the trial court imposing a sentence of death.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court held that: (1) the trial court did not commit error by denying defendant's motion for a change of venue where defendant failed to show that the jurors who actually decided his case were prejudiced; (2) the trial court did not abuse its discretion in controlling voir dire where it allowed thorough inquiry into the jurors' views, and the questions asked were sufficient to reveal any bias a prospective juror might have had; (3) the evidence supported a conviction for first degree arson under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-58, which required a finding that the dwelling was occupied, where the killing and burning were part of one continuous transaction; (4) the action of the trial court in sentencing defendant for robbery with a dangerous weapon and for two counts of larceny, lesser included offenses of the robbery, violated double jeopardy principles where they were all part of a single, continuous criminal transaction; and (5) the jury was required to give mitigating value to any statutory mitigating circumstances that it found to exist, and the trial court's instruction allowing the jury to determine whether such circumstances had mitigating value was reversible error.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for first degree murder, first degree arson, first degree burglary, and robbery with a dangerous weapon; arrested defendant's convictions for felonious larceny; vacated defendant's death sentence; and remanded for a new capital sentencing proceeding.

John R. Sexton & Co. v. Justus

December 8, 1995, Filed

DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer filed suit against defendant, the Secretary of the North Carolina Department of Revenue, seeking to recover a refund for taxes paid under protest pursuant to the Soft Drink Tax Act, N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 105-113.41 - .43 (Supp. 1994), N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 105-113.44 - .47 (1992). The COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA had affirmed a judgment entered in favor the taxpayer. The Department appealed.


OVERVIEW: The Department had conducted a Soft Drink Tax Audit of the taxpayer, a food service distribution company. By this notice, additional soft drink excise taxes were assessed against the taxpayer for sales of specific concentrated juice products covering a three-year period. The taxpayer unsuccessfully filed an objection to the assessment and an application for a hearing with the Department. The court held that a registration requirement was implicit in the Act and a relevant rule from N.C. Admin. Code tit. 17, r. 4D.0507 (July 1984). Thus, because the taxpayer failed to register with the Secretary, it was not entitled to a refund.
