State v. Bunning


No. 403A92 - Guilford


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


338 N.C. 483; 450 S.E.2d 462; 1994 N.C. LEXIS 708


April 12, 1994, Heard in the Supreme Court  
December 9, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Rousseau, J., at the 19 October 1992 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Guilford County, upon a jury verdict of guilty of first-degree murder.



DISPOSITION: IN THE GUILT PHASE: NO ERROR. IN THE PENALTY PHASE: NEW CAPITAL SENTENCING PROCEEDING.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a) of the judgment from the Criminal Session of Superior Court, Guilford County (North Carolina), that convicted him of first-degree murder and imposed a sentence of death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant raised numerous assignments of error with regarding to his conviction of first-degree murder. In finding no error in the guilt phase, the court held that the trial court's charge to the jury on reasonable doubt was not in error because the words showed that the trial court did not lessen the burden of proof beyond a reasonable doubt. The court further held that although it did not approve of the prosecuting attorney's argument that used Biblical quotations, the court believed that the jurors were able to discount the promise. The Biblical quotation used by the prosecuting attorney implied to the jurors that if defendant was guilty and they convicted him they would have been blessed by God. However, the court ordered a new capital sentencing proceeding for the error by the trial court in finding the aggravating circumstance. Defendant could not have received the death penalty for the crime to which he pled guilty in Virginia, and he did not plead guilty to a capital felony.


OUTCOME: The court found no error in the guilt phase of defendant's trial for first-degree murder but ordered a new capital sentencing proceeding in the penalty phase.

 

109 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


City of New Bern v. New Bern-Craven County Bd. of Educ.


No. 5PA94 - Craven


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


338 N.C. 430; 450 S.E.2d 735; 1994 N.C. LEXIS 704


October 12, 1994, Heard in the Supreme Court  
December 9, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. 7A-30(1) and on discretionary review pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-31 of a unanimous decision of the Court of Appeals, 113 N.C. App. 98, 437 S.E.2d 655 (1993), affirming a judgment entered 24 February 1992 by Butterfield, J., in Superior Court, Craven County.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee city filed a declaratory judgment suit, seeking to have three statutes governing the inspection of buildings in the city declared unconstitutional. Appellants, county, county education board, community college, county medical authority (county entities), and the state attorney general, challenged the order of the Court of Appeals (North Carolina) that affirmed the trial court's ruling declaring the statutes unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The county entities argued that the statutes were constitutional because they were general rather than local and, if local, they did not relate to health or sanitation and thus were permissible under N.C. Const. art. II, § 24. They contended that the city required special legislative attention because the city and the county could not agree on which entity should perform the inspections of the buildings covered by the three acts. They contended this inability to agree provided a rational basis for the legislature's objective in enacting the acts, that of designating the county to perform the inspections. The court affirmed the appellate court's decision, holding that the statutes were local. Based on N.C. Gen. Stat. § 160A-411 and the facts of the case, the court perceived no rational basis that justified the separation of the city from all other cities in the state for special legislative attention regarding the designation of an appropriate inspection department. The court also concluded that the acts that altered the selection process of those who would enforce the building code affected health and sanitation. Thus, the acts were prohibited by N.C. Const. art. II, § 24.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court, finding the three statutes governing the inspection of buildings in the city unconstitutional.

 

110 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


McDonald's Corp. v. Dwyer


No. 355PA93 - Craven


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


338 N.C. 445; 450 S.E.2d 888; 1994 N.C. LEXIS 706; 61 A.L.R.5th 927


April 11, 1994, Heard in the Supreme Court  
December 9, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On discretionary review pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-31 from a unanimous decision of the Court of Appeals, 111 N.C. App. 127, 432 S.E.2d 165 (1993), reversing an order entered 22 October 1991 by Judge David E. Reid, Jr., in Craven County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff adjacent landowner sought review from a decision of the Court of Appeals (North Carolina), which reversed an order entered in the trial court that determined that, pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-44.2, the adjacent landowner was entitled to possession of the disputed property as opposed to defendant record title holder. .


OVERVIEW: The adjacent landowner argued that the lower court erred when it determined that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-44.2 was unconstitutional in part because it failed to provide the record title holder with adequate notice, an opportunity to be heard, and just compensation. The adjacent landowner claimed that the statute provided sufficient notice because the statute in question was a self-executing statute and did not cause vested property rights to be reduced to a mere cause of action. The court rejected the adjacent landowner's arguments and affirmed the lower court. The court pointed out that due process required notice greater than that provided by operation of law prior to divestment of a vested property interest. The court noted that the disputed statutory language unconstitutionally transferred property from the record title holder to the adjacent landowner by creation of a presumption that, after a period of time, the title was taken from the record title holder, and transferred to the adjacent landowner without any notice or consideration. This presumption effectively forfeiting the record title holder's rights in a valuable property interest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the lower court, which reversed the trial court's summary judgment in favor of the adjacent landowner.

 

111 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Fulton Corp. v. Justus


No. 305A93 - Wake


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


338 N.C. 472; 450 S.E.2d 728; 1994 N.C. LEXIS 707


February 2, 1994, Heard in the Supreme Court  
December 9, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari granted Fulton Corp. v. Faulkner, 514 U.S. 1062, 131 L. Ed. 2d 554, 115 S. Ct. 1689, 1995 U.S. LEXIS 2616 (1995)
Motion denied by Fulton Corp. v. Faulkner, 515 U.S. 1156, 132 L. Ed. 2d 851, 115 S. Ct. 2637, 1995 U.S. LEXIS 4459 (1995)
Reversed by, Remanded by Fulton Corp. v. Faulkner, 516 U.S. 325, 133 L. Ed. 2d 796, 116 S. Ct. 848, 1996 U.S. LEXIS 1379 (1996)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by plaintiff and defendant as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-30(1) from a unanimous decision by the Court of Appeals, 110 N.C. App. 493, 430 S.E.2d 494 (1993), reversing summary judgment for defendant, entered by Brooks, J., on 8 November 1991 in Superior Court, Wake County. This Court also allowed plaintiff's petition for discretionary review of additional issues pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-31. n1. n1 Because of our resolution of the Commerce Clause issue against plaintiff's position, the additional issues raised in plaintiff's petition need not be addressed.
Fulton Corp. v. Justus, 110 N.C. App. 493, 430 S.E.2d 494, 1993 N.C. App. LEXIS 561 (1993)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporation taxpayer appealed a summary judgment granted defendant taxing authority, and which held that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-203 which imposed an intangibles tax on the ownership of corporate stock did not violate the Commerce Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3. The Court of Appeals (North Carolina) reversed, and based upon a severance cause in the statute struck the unconstitutional portion only. Both parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: The intangibles tax paid was imposed upon the value of the stock which was the result of computing the allocable percentage of the issuing corporation's income to North Carolina, which reduced by that percentage the value of the stock for intangibles tax purposes, and similarly reduced the intangibles tax by that percentage. By analogy to another state's intangibles tax imposed on domestic corporations that owned stock in foreign corporations that did not pay property taxes in that state as being substantially equivalent, sufficient for Commerce Clause purposes, to that state's tax on the domestic corporations' property, the court held that § 105-203 also satisfied the Commerce Clause. The court reasoned that § 105-203, in effect reduced the corporate taxpayer's intangible tax liability directly and inversely proportionally to the issuing corporation's income allocable to North Carolina, and increased the intangibles tax to the extent the issuing corporation's income was not taxed by North Carolina. Therefore, the intangibles tax compensated for the higher income taxes paid by North Carolina corporations versus those corporations that did some business in the state.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and held that the North Carolina intangibles tax levied on the ownership of corporate stock did not violate the Commerce Clause because it did not discriminate against interstate commerce and in favor of intrastate commerce. The court then remanded for further proceedings consistent with its decision.

State v. Ward


No. 158A92 - Pitt


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


338 N.C. 64; 449 S.E.2d 709; 1994 N.C. LEXIS 651


September 14, 1993, Heard in the Supreme Court  
November 3, 1994, Filed

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal of right by defendant pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Sumner, J., at the 30 March 1992 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Pitt County, upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to additional judgments imposed for conspiracy to commit murder, robbery with a dangerous weapon, and conspiracy to commit armed robbery was granted 16 March 1993. Execution stayed 29 April 1992 pending defendant's appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court, Pitt County (North Carolina), entered a judgment imposing a sentence of death on defendant following a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder. Defendant appealed by right pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty upon the theories of premeditation, lying in wait, and felony murder. He challenged his sentence on various grounds. The court held that (1) the trial court properly denied defendant's motion for a continuance because he failed to show a reasonable likelihood that pre-trial publicity affected the fairness of his trial; (2) the trial court properly denied defendant's motion for individual voir dire of prospective jurors; (3) the trial court's error in sustaining the state's objection to testimony from defendant's physician regarding the factual basis for his opinions was harmless because the same information was later admitted; (5) the trial court did not err by refusing to submit the mitigating circumstance that defendant was under the influence of mental or emotional disturbance at the time he committed the murder because the inability to control his drug habits or temper was neither mental nor emotional disturbance under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(f)(2) (1988); (6) the trial court properly allowed the state to rebut defendant's good character evidence at sentencing with evidence of prior crimes; and (7) the death sentence was not disproportionate.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the trial court's decision entering a death sentence against defendant following his conviction for first-degree murder.

State v. Moseley


No. 124A93 - Stokes


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


338 N.C. 1; 449 S.E.2d 412; 1994 N.C. LEXIS 653


September 15, 1994, Heard in the Supreme Court  
November 3, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected June 20, 1996.
Writ of certiorari denied Moseley v. North Carolina, 514 U.S. 1091, 115 S. Ct. 1815, 131 L. Ed. 2d 738, 1995 U.S. LEXIS 3025 (1995)
Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Moseley, 344 N.C. 635, 477 S.E.2d 49, 1996 N.C. LEXIS 553 (1996)
Habeas corpus proceeding at Moseley v. French, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 36164 (M.D.N.C., Apr. 13, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from judgments entered by McHugh, J., at the 25 January 1993 Special Criminal Session of Superior Court, Stokes County, imposing a sentence of death upon a jury verdict of guilty of first-degree murder and imposing consecutive sentences of life imprisonment for first-degree sexual assault and first-degree rape.
State v. Moseley, 336 N.C. 710, 445 S.E.2d 906, 1994 N.C. LEXIS 420 (1994)



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction and death sentence imposed by the Superior Court, Stokes County (North Carolina), pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a). Defendant claimed that the trial court erred when it denied his motion for a change of venue, denied his motion for funds to employ a pathologist, unconstitutionally limited the scope of defendant's voir dire, and refused to intervene ex mero motu during the prosecutors' closing arguments.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed after he was convicted of murder and the death penalty was imposed. The court affirmed defendant's conviction and the sentence imposed. The court held that the trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion for a change of venue. The court noted that defendant had not established a reasonable likelihood that pretrial publicity prevented him from receiving a fair and impartial trial. The court ruled that defendant did not demonstrate a sufficient showing of particularized need to warrant the trial court's retaining an independent expert for the defense, that the trial court did not unconstitutionally limit the scope of defendant's voir dire, did not abuse its discretion in curtailing defendant's voir dire inquiries, did not err in denying defendant's challenge for cause of prospective jurors, and did not abuse its discretion in the conducting of the jury voir dire. The court concluded that the prosecutors' arguments were not so grossly improper as to require the trial judge to intervene ex mero motu. The court held that based on the characteristics of defendant and the crimes he committed, the death sentence imposed was not excessive or disproportionate.


OUTCOME: The court found that the trial court had committed no error in denying defendant's motion for a change of venue, in conducting the voir dire, or in conducting the trial. The court found that the sentence imposed was not disproportional or imposed because of passion, prejudice, or any other arbitrary factor, and affirmed defendant's conviction and the imposition of the death penalty.

North Buncombe Ass'n of Concerned Citizens v. North Carolina Dep't of Env't, Health & Natural Resources


No. 506PA93 - Buncombe


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


338 N.C. 302; 449 S.E.2d 451; 1994 N.C. LEXIS 654


May 13, 1994, Heard In The Supreme Court  
November 3, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended November 7, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: On discretionary review of an unpublished opinion of the Court of Appeals, 112 N.C. App. 366, 437 S.E.2d 539 (1993), reversing orders by Lewis (Robert D.), J., entered at the 1 July 1991 and 3 September 1991 sessions of Superior Court, Buncombe County.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent North Carolina Department of Environment, Health, and Natural Resources (department) and intervenor mining company sought review of the judgment of the North Carolina Court of Appeals, which reversed the orders of the trial court and dismissed the action of petitioners, citizens' associations and their officials, for an order to compel a contested case hearing before the Office of Administrative Hearings (OAH).


OVERVIEW: The citizens' associations and their officials sought to challenge the department's issuance of a mining permit to the mining company in a contested case hearing before the OAH. The appellate court held that the Administrative Procedures Act (APA) did not provide a right to a contested case hearing because the Mining Act of 1971 did not provide for one. The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court. The court held that the appellate court erred in holding that the OAH lacked jurisdiction to conduct a contested case hearing on the claim of the citizens' associations and their officials because the APA granted the right to a contested case hearing to all persons aggrieved by a state agency decision unless expressly excluded by the APA or the organic act which created the right. The court found that neither the APA nor the Mining Act of 1971 expressly denied the right to a contested case hearing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court dismissing the petition of the citizens' associations and their officials for a contested case hearing on the department's issuance of a mining permit to the mining company. The court remanded the matter to the trial court for a hearing on whether the petition was timely filed and for further proceedings.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed, as of right pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a), from a judgment of the Superior Court, Edgecombe County (North Carolina), which imposed a sentence of death upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of murder in the first degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant raised numerous assignments of error on the part of the superior court during both the trial and capital punishment phases of the proceedings. Accordingly, the sentence of death imposed in the present case was determined not to be disproportionate.

Capital Outdoor Advertising v. City of Raleigh


No. 136PA93 - Wake


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


337 N.C. 150; 446 S.E.2d 289; 1994 N.C. LEXIS 500


October 13, 1993, Heard in the Supreme Court  
July 29, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On discretionary review of a unanimous decision of the Court of Appeals, 109 N.C. App. 399, 427 S.E.2d 154 (1993), vacating an order of Hight, J., dismissing plaintiffs' complaint pursuant to Rule 12(b)(6) of the North Carolina Rules of Civil Procedure, entered 4 November 1991 in Superior Court, Wake County, as a result of a motion hearing conducted at the 28 October 1991 Civil Session of Superior Court, Wake County.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (North Carolina), which vacated a trial court order dismissing as untimely under N.C. R. Civ. P. 12(b)(6) plaintiff advertisers' complaint challenging the constitutionality of a city billboard ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff advertisers received notice from defendant city demanding the removal of their nonconforming billboards by a date certain in accordance with a city ordinance. Plaintiffs filed a complaint challenging the constitutionality of the ordinance under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The trial court granted defendant's motion to dismiss on statute of limitations grounds, but entered the order out of session. The appellate court reversed and on discretionary review, the court vacated the appellate court order. The court held that because defendant's motion to dismiss did not require a jury, the trial judge was authorized to enter an order out of session. Further, plaintiffs' cause of action, for statute of limitations purposes, arose at the time defendant's billboard ordinance first became effective because it was then that the regulation injured plaintiffs' property in a clear and concrete fashion. Because plaintiffs brought their action more than three years after the ordinance became effective, their action was time-barred.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals was reversed, and the case was remanded to that court for further remand to the trial court for reinstatement of the trial judge's order dismissing plaintiff advertisers' challenge to defendant city's billboard ordinance because plaintiffs' action was time-barred.

Phelps v. Phelps


No. 144PA93 - Orange


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


337 N.C. 344; 446 S.E.2d 17; 1994 N.C. LEXIS 415; 34 A.L.R.5th 751


March 14, 1994, Heard in the Supreme Court  
July 29, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On discretionary review pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-31 of a decision of a unanimous panel of the Court of Appeals, 109 N.C. App. 242, 426 S.E.2d 294 (1993), remanding for a new trial a child custody and support order of Hunt (now Love), J., filed 30 April 1991 in District Court, Orange County, awarding sole custody of the child, Joshua Phelps, to the defendant-mother and granting substantial visitation rights to the plaintiff-father.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted discretionary review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals (North Carolina) that remanded a trial court's order that, in the parties child custody dispute, awarded defendant mother sole custody and plaintiff father visitation rights. The father claimed that the trial court violated his rights under the Equal Protection clause by awarding custody based upon the parties' ages.


OVERVIEW: Judgment was rendered for the mother in the father's suit to obtain sole custody of the parties' child. In its written judgment the trial court referenced the parties' ages in the factual findings. In the oral statement explaining the decision, the trial court mentioned the father's age and stated that the child's best interests were better served by placing him with the younger mother because she would live longer. The appellate court reversed the judgment on grounds that the trial court impermissibly treated the father's age as a fundamental reason for its decision. On discretionary review, the state supreme court ruled that the trial court properly considered the father's age and, moreover, the record established that the father's age was but one of several factors weighed by the trial court. Other factors, the court observed, included the father's alleged infidelity, drinking problem, and the parties' different preferences for the child's religious and educational training. The court ruled that the trial court did not impermissibly classify the father based upon his age nor did it apply a presumption against the older parent, in violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 50-13.2(a) (1987).


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the order. The trial court's brief reference to the father's age in its oral statement to the parties and its brief reference to the parties' ages in the factual findings in the written judgment did not amount to a classification based upon age nor a presumption against the father. The trial court properly considered the father's age as one of the relevant factors in making its custody determination.

State v. Skipper


No. 122A92 - Bladen


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


337 N.C. 1; 446 S.E.2d 252; 1994 N.C. LEXIS 419


February 1, 1994, Heard in the Supreme Court  
July 29, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied Skipper v. North Carolina, 513 U.S. 1134, 115 S. Ct. 953, 130 L. Ed. 2d 895, 1995 U.S. LEXIS 870 (1995)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from judgments imposing two sentences of death entered by Britt, J., at the 4 February 1991 Special Criminal Session of Superior Court, Bladen County.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was found guilty of two counts of first-degree murder and sentenced to death for each killing by the Special Criminal Session of Superior Court, Bladen County (North Carolina). Defendant appealed as of right pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a).


OVERVIEW: The court rejected defendant's challenges to jury selection, jury instructions, and sentencing. A juror who gave conflicting answers about the death penalty was properly excluded. Instructions that allowed the jury to infer premeditation and deliberation from a lack of provocation correctly placed the burden of proving those elements on the state. Evidence did not support an instruction on voluntary intoxication. A state statute requiring a trial court to instruct a jury during capital sentencing about parole eligibility was not in effect at the time of trial and did not apply. The trial court properly submitted as aggravating circumstances that the two murders were committed as part of a course of conduct. Despite defendant's IQ of 69, the death penalty was not unconstitutional. The death penalty was not excessive or disproportionate to the penalty imposed in similar cases.

State v. Bacon


No. 209A91 - Onslow


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


337 N.C. 66; 446 S.E.2d 542; 1994 N.C. LEXIS 426


September 8, 1992, Heard in the Supreme Court; opinion filed June 30, 1993, Withdrawn August 17, 1993 for reconsideration of proportionality review issue.  
July 29, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Fullwood, J., at the 4 February 1991 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Onslow County.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed as of right, pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a), from an order of the Criminal Session of Superior Court, Onslow County (North Carolina), which imposed a sentence of death under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000 following his conviction for first-degree murder.


OVERVIEW: The jury found one aggravating circumstance submitted against defendant, that the murder was committed for pecuniary gain. Defendant argued that his death sentence was disproportionate to other cases involving only pecuniary gain as an aggravating circumstance. In all but two of the 14 cases, the jury imposed a sentence of life imprisonment. After reviewing the resentencing phase of defendant's trial and finding that no error occurred, the court concluded that the record supported the jury's finding of the single aggravating circumstance that the murder was committed for the purpose of collecting life insurance proceeds. The court then determined that it could not hold as a matter of law that defendant's death sentence was disproportionate or excessive. Unlike the other cases involving the single aggravating circumstance of pecuniary gain, defendant had pre-planned the murder over a lengthy period of time and coldly calculated the brutal assault. The killing was unprovoked, defendant showed no remorse or pity after the murder, and he was not found to be under the influence of mental or emotional disturbance.


OUTCOME: The court found no error in defendant's sentencing proceeding. The court could not find as a matter of law that defendant's death sentence was disproportionate or excessive in comparing the case to similar cases in which the death penalty was imposed.

State v. Daniels


No. 506A90 - Mecklenburg


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


337 N.C. 243; 446 S.E.2d 298; 1994 N.C. LEXIS 428


May 13, 1994, Heard in the Supreme Court  
July 29, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Motion for Limited Reconsideration Allowed August 25, 1994. Defendant's Motion for Temporary Stay or Withdrawal of the Mandate Denied August 25, 1994, Reported at: 1994 N.C. LEXIS 501. As Amended August 26, 1994.
Amended by, On reconsideration by, in part, Reconsideration denied by, in part State v. Daniels, 1994 N.C. LEXIS 501 (N.C., Aug. 25, 1994)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Daniels v. North Carolina, 513 U.S. 1135, 130 L. Ed. 2d 895, 115 S. Ct. 953, 1995 U.S. LEXIS 871 (1995)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Fulton, J., at the 27 August 1990 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Mecklenburg County, upon a jury verdict of guilty of first-degree murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to additional judgments imposed for assault with a deadly weapon with intent to kill inflicting serious injury, two counts of assault with a deadly weapon, and attempting to burn a dwelling house was allowed 14 February 1992.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a), defendant appealed as of right a judgment from the Criminal Session of Superior Court, Mecklenburg County (North Carolina), which imposed a sentence of death upon his capital conviction for first-degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Upon its consideration of the various assignments of error concerning the guilt phase, the court concluded that defendant was properly convicted of first-degree murder. The court found that only harmless error resulted from the asserted instances of ex parte communication by the trial judge, noting that defendant's absence at each of the challenged phases of trial could not have adversely affected his opportunity to defend. The court similarly rejected as meritless defendant's challenge to the denial of his suppression motion, the refusal to allow him to refresh a witness' recollection, and the refusal to permit the testimony of the public defender. With respect to sentencing issues, the court concluded that each of the challenged jury instructions was appropriate, as they comported with the relevant law and the jury was able to follow the instructions as they were given. The court further found that none of the challenged remarks made during closing argument sufficiently prejudiced defendant as to warrant reversal. The court concluded by declining to set aside the sentence of death, as that sentence was not disproportionate to those imposed in similar cases.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction and the death sentence imposed upon defendant.

State v. Sexton


No. 499A91 - Wake


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


336 N.C. 321; 444 S.E.2d 879; 1994 N.C. LEXIS 302


April 13, 1993, Heard in the Supreme Court  
June 17, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected July 14, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Farmer, J., at the 9 September 1991 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Wake County, upon a jury verdict of guilty of first-degree murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to additional judgments imposed for first-degree kidnapping, first-degree rape, first-degree sexual offense, and common-law robbery was granted 19 October 1992.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his death sentence from the Superior Court, Wake County (North Carolina), arguing that the trial court erred in permitting the rebuttal testimony about the victim's general good moral character, devotion to family, and reputation for marital fidelity.


OVERVIEW: On defendant's appeal as of right from the sentence imposed upon a jury verdict of guilty of first-degree murder, the court held that in the limited circumstance where the rape victim was deceased and defendant's own testimony brought into question the victim's sexual behavior, the prosecution was permitted to present rebuttal evidence relating to the victim's prior sexual conduct to challenge the credibility of defendant's testimony. By attacking the victim's character for marital fidelity, defendant went beyond what was necessary for his defense and opened the door to the rebuttal evidence. Opening the door referred to the principle that where one party introduced evidence of a particular fact, the opposing party was entitled to introduce evidence in explanation or rebuttal thereof, even though the rebuttal evidence would have been incompetent or irrelevant had it been offered initially.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

State v. Morston


No. 353A92 - Hoke


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


336 N.C. 381; 445 S.E.2d 1; 1994 N.C. LEXIS 311


February 2, 1994, Heard in the Supreme Court  
June 17, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at, Remanded by State v. Morston, 147 N.C. App. 313, 556 S.E.2d 355, 2001 N.C. App. LEXIS 1161 (2001)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment entered by Ellis, J., on 28 May 1992, in the Superior Court, Hoke County, sentencing the defendant to life imprisonment for first-degree murder. The defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to additional judgments was allowed by the Supreme Court on 19 May 1993.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment and sentences of the Superior Court, Hoke County (North Carolina) for first degree murder, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a) (1989), and related crimes. He asserted errors in his conspiracy conviction, lack of intent to prove assault of a bystander, denial of request for a second degree murder verdict, and use of same evidence to prove two aggravating factors, Fair Sentencing Act, N. C. Gen. Stat. §15A-1340.4(a)(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of and sentenced for conspiracy to commit murder, first degree murder, and related offenses. He sought review, alleging error in his conviction for conspiracy and murder, insufficient evidence of intent to assault a bystander, denial of his request to submit a second degree murder verdict to the jury, and the use of the same evidence to prove two factors in aggravation. The court affirmed the convictions, but vacated the sentence for conspiracy. It found that defendant was properly convicted of, and punished for, both conspiracy and the substantive offense which he conspired to commit. Under the doctrine of transferred intent, evidence that defendant possessed the intent to shoot and kill the victim sufficed as the intent for felony assault on a bystander. There was no error in denying the request to submit a possible verdict on second degree murder to the jury. Defendant was entitled to resentencing on his conviction for conspiracy to commit murder because the trial court improperly used evidence of defendant's conspiracy as the basis for finding two factors in aggravation, contrary to the Fair Sentencing Act, N. C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-13240.4(a)(1).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for first degree murder and the accompanying life sentence. The court affirmed the convictions for conspiracy to commit murder and related offenses, but vacated the sentences imposed on those convictions because the trial court erred in utilizing the same item of evidence to prove two factors in aggravation.

State v. Worsley


No. 413A92 - Chatham


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


336 N.C. 268; 443 S.E.2d 68; 1994 N.C. LEXIS 233


November 17, 1993, Heard in the Supreme Court  
May 6, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment entered by Battle, J., on 5 June 1992, in the Superior Court, Chatham County, sentencing the defendant to life imprisonment for first-degree murder.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a person convicted of first degree murder under the felony murder rule, first-degree burglary, and attempted first-degree rape, appealed his convictions by the Superior Court, Chatham County (North Carolina), alleging that the state presented insufficient evidence to sustain his convictions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of first degree murder under the felony murder rule, first-degree burglary, and attempted first-degree rape. He appealed, arguing that the state failed to present sufficient evidence to sustain the convictions. The court found no error in the judgment. The court found that the evidence consisted of testimony by an eyewitness who said that defendant had been seen in the grassy common area of the victim's apartment complex bending over the victim and stabbing her and of testimony by police officers, who said that a large rock had been thrown through the window of the victim's apartment, that blood had been found on a couch inside as well as outside, that blood had been found on the doorknob of defendant's apartment, and that a search of defendant's apartment turned up bloody pants. The blood, in each instance, belonged to the victim. There was also evidence that semen was found in the victim's vaginal area and on her underpants, although the semen was never confirmed to have been defendant's. It could have been inferred from this evidence, the court ruled, that defendant broke into the victim's apartment with the intent to at least rape her.


OUTCOME: The court found no error in a judgment that convicted defendant of first degree murder under the felony murder rule, first-degree burglary, and attempted first-degree rape because the state presented evidence that tended to show that defendant had thrown a rock through the victim's window, that there was semen present on the victim, and that defendant had stabbed the victim on a couch insider her apartment and in a grassy common area outside.

State v. Green


385A84-3 - Pitt


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


336 N.C. 142; 443 S.E.2d 14; 1994 N.C. LEXIS 241


November 16, 1993, Heard in the Supreme Court  
May 6, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal of right by the defendant pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from two judgments imposing sentences of death entered by Brown, J., on 3 September 1992 in the Superior Court, Pitt County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Pitt County (North Carolina), which found him guilty of murder and sentenced him to death. He claimed that voir dire was improper, that the jury instructions were erroneous, that the prosecutor committed misconduct during closing arguments, and that the death penalty was not in proportion to the crime charged.


OVERVIEW: Defendant shot and killed two people. He was convicted of murder and given the death penalty. Defendant challenged his conviction, arguing that voir dire was improper, the jury instructions were erroneous, the prosecutor committed misconduct during closing arguments, and the death penalty was not in proportion to the crime charged. The court affirmed, holding that the trial court properly excused prospective jurors for cause on the basis of their opposition to the death penalty. The court also determined that, while the prosecutor made improper comments during closing arguments, his argument was not so grossly improper as to require the trial court to intervene. The court further stated that the jury instructions gave the correct statement of law, and that the conviction was lawfully obtained by the prosecutor. Finally, the court concluded that defendant's sentence of death was in proportion to his crime, and after making an independent evaluation of the facts, was in line with other cases in which the death penalty was assessed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction, holding that the trial court did not commit reversible error during voir dire by excusing prospective jurors for cause on the basis of their opposition to the death penalty, that the prosecutor's argument was not so grossly improper as to require the trial court to intervene, and that defendant's sentence of death was in proportion to his crime and other death penalty cases.

State v. Jones


No. 395A91 - Wake


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


336 N.C. 229; 443 S.E.2d 48; 1994 N.C. LEXIS 242


September 16, 1993, Heard in the Supreme Court  
May 6, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Farmer. J., at the 1 July 1991 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Wake County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision from the Superior Court, Wake County (North Carolina), which convicted him of murder and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant made various contentions of error on appeal, however the court held them to be without merit. The court held that the trial court did not commit reversible error even though it denied defendant's request to interrogate the jurors as to whether they could follow an instruction that a life sentence actually meant a life sentence that was not subject to parole. The court held that defendant had the right to such an interrogation, however the trial court's error did not amount to a prejudicial error because all of the selected jurors were present when two potential jurors indicated that they felt that a life sentence meant that defendant would eventually be paroled. The court held that because the selected jurors did not indicate that they agreed with the two potential jurors that were excused, it was probable that they did not consider the possibility of parole in their ultimate decision to impose the death penalty. The court held that videotape of defendant committing a robbery in conjunction with the murder was properly admitted even though defendant had pled guilty to the robbery because the video was relevant to show defendant's motive for the murder.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court because defendant's various contentions of error were without merit.

State v. Robinson


No. 273A92 - Guilford


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


336 N.C. 78; 443 S.E.2d 306; 1994 N.C. LEXIS 229



May 6, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied Robinson v. North Carolina, 513 U.S. 1089, 115 S. Ct. 750, 130 L. Ed. 2d 650, 1995 U.S. LEXIS 276 (1995)
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed, Summary judgment granted by, Motion denied by, Certificate of appealability denied Robinson v. Vaughn, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 57316 (M.D.N.C., May 1, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Ross, J., at the 18 May 1992 Special Criminal Session of Superior Court, Guilford County. Heard in the Supreme Court 8 December 1993.
State v. Robinson, 330 N.C. 1, 409 S.E.2d 288, 1991 N.C. LEXIS 666 (1991)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order of the Superior Court (North Carolina), which sentenced him to death for first-degree murder. On defendant's direct appeal, the court had affirmed his convictions for first-degree murder, robbery with a dangerous weapon, and two counts of assault with a deadly weapon with intent to kill inflicting serious injury, but vacated the death sentence and remanded for a new capital sentencing proceeding.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was sentenced to death for first-degree murder. On direct appeal, his convictions for murder, robbery, and assault were affirmed, but his death sentence was vacated and remanded. On review, the court found that the state used peremptory challenges in a race-neutral way. The state's objections as to form of questions about whether prospective jurors could consider a life sentence if they found that mitigating outweighed aggravating circumstances were sustained because, among other things, some of the questions were not properly phrased. The trial court properly excused a juror for cause where her responses indicated that her feelings about the death penalty would prevent her from following the law. It was error to refuse to submit the mitigating circumstance that in prison, defendant could abide by the rules, but any error was harmless as to the failure to submit the circumstance because a different result would not have been reached at sentencing. Objections to jury instructions were overruled for reasons including that the instructions were a correct statement of the law of mitigation. Finding no error, the court affirmed the sentence, holding it was proportionate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the death sentence for first degree murder. Among other things, the state used peremptory challenges in a race-neutral way, the state properly objected to incorrectly phrased questions, it was appropriate to excuse a juror for cause where her feelings about the death penalty would prevent her from following the law, and the sentence was proportionate as compared to other cases.

State v. Moore

SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court, Forsyth County (North Carolina), which found her guilty of first-degree murder and sentenced her to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's father, husband, and boyfriend all died from arsenic poisoning. Defendant was sentenced to death for the murder of her boyfriend. The court rejected her argument that the trial court erroneously denied her motion for a change of venue, as she did not establish a reasonable likelihood that pretrial publicity prevented her from receiving a fair trial. The court also found that defendant's sentence was proportionate, and the aggravating circumstances of a heinous act for pecuniary gain were appropriate.

Swanson v. State


64PA91 - Wake


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


335 N.C. 674; 441 S.E.2d 537; 1994 N.C. LEXIS 111; 62 U.S.L.W. 2576; 18 Employee Benefits Cas. (BNA) 1303


November 17, 1993, Heard in the Supreme Court  
March 4, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On remand from the United States Supreme Court.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the State and the state secretary of revenue, challenged the decision of the trial court, which entered summary judgment in favor of appellees, former federal employees and active duty federal military personnel and reservists, in their action for the refund of certain income taxes. The matter was before the court upon remand from the U.S. Supreme Court.


OVERVIEW: The former federal employees and active duty federal military personnel and reservists contended they were entitled to full refunds for income taxes paid by them on pension benefits for the tax years 1985 through 1988 because the state statute exempting state employees' retirement benefits from taxation but not granting the same exemption to their federal counterparts violated the constitutional doctrines of intergovernmental tax immunity and 4 U.S.C.S. § 111. The court concluded the former federal employees and active duty federal military personnel and reservists were procedurally barred from recovering the refunds sought because they failed to comply with the state's statutory postpayment refund demand procedure, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-267 (1992). The court further held that the refund procedure provided in § 105-267 was free from any constitutional infirmity and that it could, therefore, effectively bar actions for refunds in the tax years for which the procedure was not followed. The procedure comported with due process.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the entry of summary judgment for the former federal employees and active duty federal military personnel and reservists entered by the trial court and remanded for entry of judgment for the State and the state secretary of revenue dismissing the action.

State v. Patterson


No. 29A93 - Halifax


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


335 N.C. 437; 439 S.E.2d 578; 1994 N.C. LEXIS 1


September 17, 1993, Heard in the Supreme Court  
January 28, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal of right by defendant pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of life imprisonment entered by Allsbrook, J., at the 2 August 1992 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Halifax County, upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction for first degree murder entered by the Superior Court, Halifax County (North Carolina). Defendant claimed that his right to a fair trial was violated.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder and challenged his conviction claiming that he was denied a fair trial because the jury was improperly instructed. The court rejected defendant's argument finding that it was not "likely" that the jury had convicted him on a standard less than reasonable doubt. The court found that it was only a "possibility" that the jury had viewed the term "moral certainty" as a standard of proof less than a reasonable doubt. Defendant also argued that the trial court improperly refused to add to the definition of reasonable doubt certain verbatim language. The court rejected this argument finding that the trial court was not required to include the requested instructions verbatim and it was sufficient that it gave them in substance. Further, the court found that the trial court had made it "overly clear" that the jury could acquit defendant if it found that the prosecution failed to prove its case beyond a reasonable doubt. Finally, the court found that the trial court had properly denied defendant's motion to dismiss because there was sufficient evidence to sustain a verdict of first-degree murder.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for first-degree murder finding that he received a fair trial, free from prejudicial error.

State v. Rose


No. 182A92 - Haywood


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


335 N.C. 301; 439 S.E.2d 518; 1994 N.C. LEXIS 7


September 15, 1993, Heard in the Supreme Court  
January 28, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Allen (C. Walter), J., at the 4 May 1992 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Haywood County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Haywood County (North Carolina), which held that defendant was guilty of first degree murder and sentenced defendant to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder and sentenced to death. Defendant appealed pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a) arguing that the trial court erred by admitting evidence that he burned the victim's body a day later because the evidence was not relevant to prove premeditation or deliberation. The court disagreed and affirmed the conviction and sentence. The court held that while there was no direct evidence of premeditation and deliberation, there was evidence of an elaborate process of covering up the crime and that the handling of the body was evidence from which a jury could have inferred premeditation and deliberation. The court also rejected defendant's contention that the trial court erred in admitting autopsy photographs, holding that although some of the photos were gruesome, they were relevant to illustrate the testimony of the pathologist and were illustrative of the nature of the victim's wounds. The court held that the admission into evidence of knives taken from defendant's residence were relevant and that the trial court did not err by refusing to grant defendant's motion for a directed verdict and in instructing the jury on the definition of malice.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of first degree murder and the imposition of the death penalty. The court rejected defendant's numerous allegations of error and concluded that the guilt and sentencing phases of defendant's trial were free from prejudicial error and that the sentence of death was neither disproportionate nor imposed under the influence of passion, prejudice, or any other arbitrary factor.

State v. Carter


No. 436A92 - Scotland


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


335 N.C. 422; 440 S.E.2d 268; 1994 N.C. LEXIS 14



January 28, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of life imprisonment entered by Hobgood, J., at the 21 November 1991 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Scotland County, upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder. On 16 April 1993 this Court allowed defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to an additional judgment of imprisonment entered upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of second-degree murder. Presented before the Supreme Court on 13 October 1993 for decision upon written briefs without oral argument pursuant to Rule 30(d) of the North Carolina Rules of Appellate Procedure.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from judgments of the Superior Court of Scotland County (North Carolina) which convicted and sentenced him for first-degree murder and second-degree murder upon jury verdicts.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder and second-degree murder. On appeal, the court affirmed. Defendant exhausted his peremptory challenges but did not renew his challenge for cause. He thus foreclosed appeal of the trial court's denial of a challenge for cause. Even if the trial court's denial of his challenge for cause had been an error, it was harmless because defendant used either peremptory challenges or challenges for cause to remove all jurors whom he deemed unacceptable. The state was not bound by an exculpatory statement that it introduced if there was other evidence tending to throw a different light on the circumstances of the homicide. Such other evidence included the absence of any knives the victim could have used, defendant's previous threat to shoot a man he saw in the house, and defendant's initial statements that he killed two people, which did not include any self-defense claim. Assuming that the gun was inadmissible as unconstitutionally seized, its admission was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt because defendant admitted several times that he shot two people and because his defense was self-defense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments convicting and sentencing defendant for first-degree murder and second-degree murder.

State v. Lee

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Lamm, J., at the 26 April 1990 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Avery County, upon a sentence recommendation by the jury after the defendant pled guilty to first degree murder.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed as of right pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment of the Criminal Session of Superior Court, Avery County (North Carolina) imposing a sentence of death upon a sentence recommendation by the jury after the defendant pled guilty to first degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the trial court's imposition of a death sentence upon the jury's recommendation after defendant pled guilty to murder. The jury found aggravating circumstances in that defendant's crime was heinous, atrocious, or cruel where the victim died a slow and painful death, was kidnapped, beaten and sexually assaulted by defendant, and the murder was part of a course of conduct in which defendant engaged and included similar crimes of violence against women. The jury did find mitigating circumstances in defendant's law abiding history and medical testimony regarding defendant's brain damage from an aneurysm and subsequent surgery. The court rejected defendant's claims of error regarding mental examinations provided to the state, unrecorded bench conferences, the denial of individual voir dire, denial of requested jury instructions regarding life imprisonment and the death penalty, and the admission of victim photographs, victim impact statements and defendant's statements of other crimes.

The court held that the evidence supported the jury's findings of aggravating circumstances where the victim was kidnapped and sexually assaulted, and the crime was part of a course of conduct of similar crimes by defendant.

Nelson v. Battle Forest Friends Meeting


No. 87A93 - Guilford


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA

September 15, 1993, Heard In The Supreme Court  
November 5, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Dissenting Opinion also Reported at: 438 S.E.2d 718.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal by the defendants pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-30(2) and on discretionary review pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-31(c) from the decision of a divided panel of the Court of Appeals, 108 N.C. App. 641, 425 S.E.2d 4 (1993), reversing a judgment entered by Allen (W. Steven), J., on 24 January 1991 in Superior Court, Guilford County.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of a judgment of the Court of Appeals (North Carolina), which interpreted N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-44.2 to mean that a public road right-of-way that was entirely within a railroad easement "adjoined" the railroad easement.


OVERVIEW: N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-44.2 provided that where a railroad easement adjoined a public road right-of-way, the adjacent property owner's right, title, and interest in the abandoned railroad easement extended to the nearest edge of the public road right-of-way. Defendants contended that a public road right-of-way that was entirely within a railroad easement adjoined the abandoned railroad easement within the meaning of § 1-44.2. On appeal, the court held that plaintiffs' land was not covered by § 1-44.2 because the definition of "adjoin" did not include a tract which was included within the bounds of another tract.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment holding that the public road right-of-way adjoined the abandoned railroad easement.

 

136 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. Gibbs


No. 1A92 - Beaufort


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


335 N.C. 1; 436 S.E.2d 321; 1993 N.C. LEXIS 542


March 15, 1993, Heard In The Supreme Court  
November 5, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected December 3, 1993. As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from judgments imposing three death sentences entered by Beaty, J., at the 21 October 1991 Special Session of Superior Court, Beaufort County, on jury verdicts finding defendant guilty of three counts of first-degree murder. Execution was stayed 6 January 1992 by this Court pending defendant's appeal. Defendant having also been found guilty of one count each of first-degree burglary, conspiracy to commit first-degree murder, and conspiracy to commit first-degree burglary, the trial court consolidated these three offenses for judgment and imposed a term of imprisonment of fifty years. On 9 September 1992 this Court granted defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to his two convictions of conspiracy and his conviction of first-degree burglary.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court, Beaufort County (North Carolina), which convicted defendant of three counts of capital murder for the killings of his in-laws, as well as first-degree burglary and conspiracy to commit both burglary and murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, his girl friend, and her sister all agreed to kill defendant's in-laws. Defendant killed them and burglarized their home. Defendant was charged with and convicted of murder and sentenced to death. The court affirmed the conviction and sentence. The court did not err in excusing jurors who were opposed the death penalty, in denying defendant an opportunity to rehabilitate panelists similarly opposed, or in refusing to suppress defendant's statements due to a lack of counsel. Defendant was given a proper first appearance pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-601. Breaking and entering need not be submitted as a lesser included offense of burglary, and no error in failing to submit that defendant had no significant history of prior criminal activity. Defense counsel told the court they were avoiding it for strategic purposes. There was no duplication in submitting aggravating factor questions. There was sufficient evidence to support a submission that the murder was especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel. There was no error in the prosecutor arguing in favor of death so defendant could not commit more crimes, or in referring to the Sixth Commandment in his argument.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and death sentence. The court held that there were no errors in voir dire, the right to counsel during interrogation was not violated, defendant received his statutory first appearance in sufficient time, there was sufficient evidence to convict for both conspiracy to commit murder and burglary, no need for a lesser included instruction on breaking and entering, and no error in the charge or argument.

Collins & Aikman Corp. v. Hartford Accident & Indem. Co.

In addition, the term "penalty" as used in the policy was at best ambiguous, and should have been interpreted against defendants who wrote the policy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the reversal and remand, as North Carolina had a close connection with the interests insured in this case, and therefore state law applied. In addition, the term "penalty" as used in the policy was at best ambiguous, and should have been interpreted against defendants, who wrote the policy.

State v. McHone


No. 148A91


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA

October 8, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied McHone v. North Carolina, 511 U.S. 1046, 128 L. Ed. 2d 220, 114 S. Ct. 1577, 1994 U.S. LEXIS 3112 (1994)
Writ of habeas corpus denied McHone v. Polk, 2004 U.S. App. LEXIS 26918 (4th Cir. N.C., Dec. 28, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from judgments imposing sentences of death entered by Long, J., at the 25 February 1991 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Surry County. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to an additional judgment allowed by the Supreme Court 13 May 1992.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The defendant appealed the decision from the Superior Court, Surry County (North Carolina), which, following a jury trial, found the defendant guilty of the first-degree murder of his mother and stepfather. The trial court also found the defendant guilty of assault with a deadly weapon with intent to kill his half-brother. The jury recommended and the trial court imposed the death sentence for each of the first-degree murder convictions.


OVERVIEW: The state presented evidence tending to show that the defendant's half-brother wrestled a pistol away from the defendant. The defendant then returned with a shotgun and shot the stepfather in the chest and injured his half-brother. The defendant's mother sustained a gunshot wound to the head. The defendant presented witnesses that testified he was under the influence of drugs and alcohol at the time of the shootings. The court affirmed, holding that: (1) statements made by the mother to three persons that she was afraid the defendant was going to kill her were properly admitted under N.C. R. Evid. 803(3); (2) the jury's request to be reinstructed on certain elements of the law of murder as it applied to the mother and stepfather was specific, and the trial court reinstructed accordingly; (3) the arguments of the prosecutors in the guilt and sentencing phases of the trial were not so grossly improper as to constitute a denial of the defendant's due process rights; (4) no rational trier of fact could have found the defendant had no significant prior criminal history; and (5) the death sentences recommended were not disproportionate to penalties imposed in similar cases.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the defendant's conviction and sentence.

State v. Gay


No. 363A91


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


334 N.C. 467; 434 S.E.2d 840; 1993 N.C. LEXIS 392


January 13, 1993, Heard in the Supreme Court  
September 10, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from three judgments imposing sentences of death entered by Weeks, J., at the 8 July 1991 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Beaufort County. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to additional judgments allowed by the Supreme Court 24 June 1992.



DISPOSITION: GUILT PHASE, NO ERROR. SENTENCING PHASE, NEW SENTENCING PROCEEDING.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by jury in Superior Court, Beaufort County (North Carolina) of first degree murder, first-degree burglary, conspiracy to commit first-degree burglary, and conspiracy to commit murder. Defendant was sentenced to death. Defendant appealed convictions and sentence pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant alleged numerous prejudicial errors. She contended that there were errors in jury selection; constitutional violations based on the trial judge's ex-parte communications with the jury; inadequate jury instructions that failed to include her defense theories; and erroneous exclusion of peremptory instructions on mitigating circumstances during the sentencing phase. The supreme court affirmed the verdicts, but remanded the case for a new sentencing proceeding. The court concluded (1) that the trial judge erred when he addressed prospective jurors outside the presence of the defendant, but that the error was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt. (2) The defendant declined the opportunity for instructions on lesser-included offenses and could not claim on appeal that failure to instruct on the lesser-included offense was error. (3) The trial court committed reversible error when it failed to give a peremptory instruction for mitigating circumstances that were supported by uncontroverted evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions, but remanded the case for a new capital sentencing proceeding.

State v. Williams


No. 264A90


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


334 N.C. 440; 434 S.E.2d 588; 1993 N.C. LEXIS 394


February 10, 1992, Heard in the Supreme Court  
September 10, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Butterfield, J., at the 30 April 1990 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Wayne County, upon a jury verdict of first-degree murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to his conviction of burglary by explosives was allowed by this Court on 13 August 1991.



DISPOSITION: NEW TRIAL.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder, burglary with explosives, and attempted safe-cracking by the Superior Court of Wayne County (North Carolina). Defendant was sentenced to death. Defendant brought an appeal raising numerous assignments of error spanning both the guilt-innocence and the sentencing phases of the trial.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the trial court's jury instruction on the meaning of reasonable doubt was violative of the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment and warranted a new trial. The court rejected defendant's contention that the trial court erred in denying the motion to dismiss the first-degree murder charge holding that, viewed in the light most favorable to the State, the evidence was clearly sufficient to support a conclusion that the murder was premeditated and deliberate. Further, the court held that there was not any evidence to show that defendant was provoked. The court held that the trial court did not err in instructing the jury on felony murder because the crime of burglary with explosives, as defined N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-57, was a "burglary" within the purview of the felony murder statute. The court rejected defendant's contention that he was denied the right to represent himself, holding that defendant did not request to proceed pro se with sufficient clarity. The court held that because the right was not properly asserted, it could not have been infringed.


OUTCOME: The court ordered a new trial.

 

141 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


County of Lancaster v. Mecklenburg County


No. 293PA92


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


334 N.C. 496; 434 S.E.2d 604; 1993 N.C. LEXIS 403


January 13, 1993, Heard in the Supreme Court  
September 10, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On discretionary review of a decision of the Court of Appeals, 106 N.C. App. 646, 417 S.E.2d 827 (1992), reversing the judgment entered by Fulton, J., in the Superior Court, Mecklenburg County, on 17 January 1991 and remanding the case for further proceedings.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, neighboring county and its zoning administrator, appealed a decision of the Court of Appeals (North Carolina), which reversed a decision granting summary judgment in favor of plaintiff county and declaring a landfill zoning ordinance unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: A county and individuals filed an action for declaratory relief, seeking to have a neighboring county's zoning ordinance declared unconstitutional. The court of appeals reversed the judgment finding the ordinance unconstitutional and, on appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the decision of whether to permit a sanitary landfill was an administrative zoning decision. The county's grant of a permit application that passed the ordinance county was an administrative zoning decision that the county zoning administrator could properly make. It was not an impermissible conflict of interest because the applicant for the permit was the county itself. The same logic that permitted the county commissioners to delegate authority to the zoning administrator to issue special use permits also supported the decision that the commissioners had authority to allow the zoning administrator to issue permits for uses by right subject to special requirements.


OUTCOME: The judgment reversing the decision finding the county landfill ordinance unconstitutional was affirmed because it was a valid legislative prerogative to change the sanitary landfill use from a "special use permit" category to a "use by right under prescribed conditions" category. The ordinance, which allowed the zoning administrator to approve the permit application for siting of a landfill, was constitutional and lawful on its face.

State ex rel. Thornburg v. House & Lot


No. 150PA92


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


334 N.C. 290; 432 S.E.2d 684; 1993 N.C. LEXIS 335


October 8, 1992, Heard in the Supreme Court  
July 30, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected December 15, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: On discretionary review pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-31(b) prior to review by the Court of Appeals of order entered by Butterfield, J., in the 4 November 1991 Civil Session of Superior Court, Craven County.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Intervening defendant school board appealed the decision of the Superior Court, Craven County (North Carolina), which granted summary judgment in favor of plaintiff state in a forfeiture proceeding brought against defendant property.


OVERVIEW: A forfeiture proceeding was brought by the state, pursuant to the North Carolina Racketeer Influenced and Corrupt Organizations Act (RICO Act). The property owner agreed to a forfeiture after she was found guilty of six separate drug charges stemming form drug sales in the home. The property was sold and the board claimed a right to the proceeds. The trial court concluded that N.C. Const. art. IX, § 7 had no application to the RICO Act forfeitures and proceeds from the property sale. The trial court granted the state's motion for summary judgment. The court reversed and held that § 7 required that clear proceeds accruing to the state from any forfeiture must be paid to the public school fund. The RICO Act provided that proceeds from the sale of RICO Act forfeited property accrued to the state and those proceeds must be paid to the public school fund. Therefore, the trial court erred when it granted summary judgment in favor of the state.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the summary judgment granted to the state and required the forfeiture proceeds to be paid to the board.

State v. McCollum


No. 2A92


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


334 N.C. 208; 433 S.E.2d 144; 1993 N.C. LEXIS 342


February 15, 1993, Heard in the Supreme Court  
July 30, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment and sentence of death upon the defendant's conviction of first-degree murder, entered by Thompson, J., in the Superior Court, Cumberland County, on 22 November 1991.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with the first-degree murder and first-degree rape of a child, and the jury found him guilty. The Superior Court of Cumberland County (North Carolina) sentenced him to death, and defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant raped a child and then killed her to prevent her from telling the police. Defendant claimed that the trial judge erred in submitting to the jury the aggravating factors that the murder was committed for the purpose of preventing a lawful arrest and that the murder was especially heinous or cruel. Defendant also claimed that his rights under the Eighth Amendment were violated by the jury's failure to find the mitigating factor that his capacity to appreciate the criminality of his conduct or to conform to the requirements of law was impaired. He claimed that because the judge gave to the jury a peremptory instruction inviting it to find the mitigating factor, his rights were violated by the jury's failure to find it. The court upheld defendant's conviction and the imposition of the death penalty. The court found that (1) the submission of the aggravating factors was warranted by the evidence; (2) the jury was not required to find the mitigating factor merely because the judge gave a peremptory instruction; and (3) the jury was free not to find the mitigating factor if it did not believe that defendant's proffered evidence was credible.


OUTCOME: The court upheld defendant's conviction and the imposition of the death penalty.

State v. Petersilie

DISPOSITION: REVERSED; REMANDED; NEW TRIAL.

Dunn v. Pate


No. 170PA92


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


334 N.C. 115; 431 S.E.2d 178; 1993 N.C. LEXIS 295


January 15, 1993, Heard in the Supreme Court  
July 2, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal and discretionary review of the decision of the Court of Appeals, 106 N.C. App. 56, 415 S.E.2d 102 (1992), reversing an order granting summary judgment in favor of defendants entered by Herring, J., in the Superior Court, Cumberland County, on 31 December 1990.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, the first group of descendants, sought review of the decision from the Court of Appeals (North Carolina), which reversed an order that had granted summary judgment in favor of defendant, the second group of descendants. The first group of descendants challenged a 1962 deed as ineffective in conveying property due to failure to comply with the private examination statutes, N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 52-12, 47-39.


OVERVIEW: The first group of descendants asserted the 1962 deed was ineffective to convey the property due to noncompliance with the private examination statutes in effect at the time of conveyance. The first group claimed instead that title continued to both the husband and wife as tenants by the entirely and that upon the husband's death the property passed to the wife individually by operation of law. In such case, the first group argued, the property descended upon the wife's death by virtue of her intestacy to the five children in five equal parts rather than passing in six shares as directed by the husband's will and codicil. On appeal, the court ruled the second group of descendants had standing to challenge the statutes in question. The court noted the examination requirement allowed a wife to void a transaction into which she otherwise competently had entered on the sole basis that the contract was not certified by the clerk of the court. The court ruled the private examination statutes required unequal application of the law at the time the 1962 deed was executed and were unenforceable.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the appellate court. The court held that the private examination statutes were unconstitutional. The court ruled summary judgment for the second group of descendants was appropriate.

State v. Jennings


No. 555A90


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


333 N.C. 579; 430 S.E.2d 188; 1993 N.C. LEXIS 229


February 13, 1992, Heard in the Supreme Court  
June 4, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Butterfield, J., at the 8 October 1990 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Wilson County, upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder. Execution stayed 26 November 1990 pending defendant's appeal.



DISPOSITION: NO ERROR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court, Wilson County (North Carolina), entered a judgment imposing a sentence of death upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first degree murder based upon theories of premeditation and deliberation as well as torture. Defendant challenged her conviction and sentence.


OVERVIEW: A jury found defendant guilty of first degree murder and, following a sentencing proceeding pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000, recommended that she be sentenced to death. Defendant raised issues concerning jury selection and the guilt and sentencing phases of her trial. On appeal, the court held that: (1) the trial court properly excused a prospective juror for cause due to her views on the death penalty; (2) the forensic pathologist's testimony that the victim's injuries were consistent with "torture" and "sexual assault" was admissible because those terms were not legal terms of art which carried specific meanings not readily apparent to the witness; (3) testimony regarding the victim's good character was admissible as rebuttal evidence; (4) the record supported the jury's finding that the murder was committed while defendant was engaged in the commission of or an attempt to commit a sex offense, was committed for pecuniary gain, and was especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel; and (5) comparing the case to similar cases in which the death penalty was imposed, and in considering the crime and defendant, the death penalty was not disproportionate or excessive.
