North Carolina Ass'n of Elec. Tax Filers v. Graham


No. 228PA92


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


333 N.C. 555; 429 S.E.2d 544; 1993 N.C. LEXIS 184


January 13, 1993, Heard in the Supreme Court  
May 7, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On discretionary review pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-31 prior to a determination by the Court of Appeals of summary judgment for defendant entered by Stephens, J., at the 12 February 1992 Civil Session of Superior Court, Wake County.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporations filed suit for a declaratory judgment asserting that the Refund Anticipation Loan Act, N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 53-245 to 53-254 violated the Supremacy Clause and the Commerce Clause of the United States Constitution. The Superior Court, Wake County (North Carolina), denied the corporations' motion for summary judgment and entered summary judgment in favor of defendant state bank commissioner. The corporations sought review.


OVERVIEW: The corporations argued that Congress legislated so comprehensively that it had left no room for supplementary state legislation on refund anticipation loans (RAL). The corporations cited 26 U.S.C.S. § 6011(e) and Rev. Proc. 90-62, 1990-2 C.B. at 663 in support of their argument. The corporations also argued that the Refund Anticipation Loan Act violated the Supremacy Clause because it infringed upon and interfered with business operations of out-of-state banks. The court rejected the corporations' arguments. The court held that 26 U.S.C.S. § 6011(e) pertained only to electronic filing. Further, Rev. Proc. 90-62, 1990-2 C.B. at 663 was superseded by Rev. Proc. 91-69, 1991-2 C.B. at 897. Neither authority cited by the corporations specifically mentioned RALs. Moreover, revenue procedures were without legal effect in Supremacy Clause challenges. The court found that the Refund Anticipation Loan Act did not place an undue burden on interstate commerce. The court ruled that the burden on interstate commerce was only incidental, that the burden did not exceed the local benefits, and no pervasive federal regulatory scheme existed for RALs.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

In re Foreclosure of Deed of Trust of Michael Weinman Assoc. General Partnership


No. 430A91


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


333 N.C. 221; 424 S.E.2d 385; 1993 N.C. LEXIS 13


April 14, 1992, Heard in the Supreme Court  
January 8, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by petitioner as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-30(2) from the decision of a divided panel of the Court of Appeals, 103 N.C. App. 756, 407 S.E.2d 288 (1991), which affirmed a judgment entered for respondent by Griffin, J., at the 20 August 1990 Civil Session of Superior Court, Mecklenburg County.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant lender sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court, Mecklenburg County (North Carolina) that had entered judgment in favor of appellee mortgagor.


OVERVIEW: The issue before the court was the extent of authority and responsibility of the Clerks of Superior Court in their consideration and determination of the four elements that had to exist under the power of sale foreclosure statute, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 45-21.16(d) (1991), before foreclosure may commence. The lender had attempted foreclosure on the property of the mortgagor. The court disagreed with this contention and affirmed. The court stated that in order for a trustee under a deed of trust to have any right to foreclose on a parcel of land the deed of trust must encompass the subject property as security for the debt owed by the mortgagor.

State v. Heatwole

State v. Bronson


No. 123A92


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


333 N.C. 67; 423 S.E.2d 772; 1992 N.C. LEXIS 664


October 8, 1992, Heard in the Supreme Court  
December 18, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from judgment imposing sentence of life imprisonment entered by Stevens (Henry L., III), J., at the 15 July 1991 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Pender County.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed, as of right pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a), from a judgment entered by the Criminal Session of Superior Court, Pender County (North Carolina), which imposed a sentence of life imprisonment. Defendant was found guilty of first-degree murder of his wife.


OVERVIEW: Defendant gave several versions of what happened the night his wife was shot. Most of the versions dealt with an intruder who had allegedly entered the house and shot the wife. However, the evidence did not reveal the presence of an intruder. Defendant then admitted killing his wife, but denied that he had planned to kill her. The court found no prejudicial error in the conviction and affirmed the imposition of life imprisonment. Defendant did not show plain error as to jury instructions given because, even if the jurors had been told about the qualifications of one witness, the jury would have reached the same result. Defendant's failure to object to questions by the prosecutor meant waiver of the attorney-client privilege, which defendant did not raise. Nor was plain error committed when defendant's neighbor was allowed to testify that defendant's wife constantly complained and belittled defendant because defendant did not meet the burden of proving that the cross-examination resulted in an error so grave as to cause the jury to reach a decision it would not have reached absent the testimony. Finally, a life imprisonment sentence did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that defendant received a fair trial free of prejudicial error and affirmed the life sentence.

 

152 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. Cummings


No. 48A88


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


332 N.C. 487; 422 S.E.2d 692; 1992 N.C. LEXIS 587


November 14, 1991, Heard in the Supreme Court  
November 19, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Herring, J., at the 7 November 1987 Criminal Session of Superior Court, New Hanover County.



DISPOSITION: GUILT-INNOCENCE PHASE: NO ERROR. SENTENCING PROCEEDING: NEW CAPITAL SENTENCING PROCEEDING.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision of the Criminal Session of the Superior Court, New Hanover County (North Carolina), which imposed a sentence of death after his conviction for first-degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of first-degree murder, and was sentenced to death. On appeal, he argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred in its instructions during sentencing, and several unrecorded bench conferences held outside his presence violated his rights. The court affirmed the conviction, but vacated the death sentence and remanded for a new capital sentencing because of an error in instructions that was not harmless beyond a reasonable doubt. The court held that the bench conferences did not violate defendant's rights because they were attended by his attorneys, and it was not necessary to record bench conferences between trial judges and attorneys. The court found that the trial court gave an unconstitutional instruction that required the jury to find mitigating circumstance unanimously, and the state did not demonstrate that the error was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt, so the sentencing proceeding was constitutionally infirm and the instruction might have prevented one or more jurors from finding mitigating circumstance existed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction but reversed and remanded the death sentence because the trial court gave an improper and unconstitutional instruction that required the jury to find mitigating circumstance unanimously. The sentencing was constitutionally infirm because the instruction might have prevented jurors from finding mitigating circumstances.

State v. Hucks


No. 157A92


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


332 N.C. 650; 422 S.E.2d 711; 1992 N.C. LEXIS 592


October 8, 1992, Submitted to the Supreme Court without oral argument, by motion of the parties, pursuant to Rule 30(d) of the North Carolina Rules of Appellate Procedure.  
November 19, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from judgment entered by Bowen, J., on 4 December 1991, in the Superior Court, Robeson County, sentencing the defendant to life imprisonment for murder in the first degree. Submitted to the Supreme Court on 8 October 1992 without oral argument, by motion of the parties, pursuant to Rule 30(d) of the North Carolina Rules of Appellate Procedure.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed as of right under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a) an order of the Robeson County Superior Court (North Carolina), which convicted him of murder in the first degree and sentenced him to life imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: The victim called his wife frequently because she was recovering from a heart attack. Eyewitnesses saw defendant and his accomplice enter the victim's jewelry store, and found them in the store shortly before the body was discovered. At trial, the wife's testimony of when her husband had last called her and why he called frequently was admitted. The trial court convicted defendant of capital murder, but the conviction was reversed and the case remanded. Following a new trial, defendant was convicted and sentenced. Defendant appealed. The court heard the appeal without oral argument and affirmed. The medical condition of the victim's wife was relevant under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 8C-1, N.C. R. Evid. 401 (1988) because it showed why she and victim communicated and caused her to know after what point in time he was killed. Because defendant failed to challenge the eyewitness identification as having been unconstitutionally suggestive by one of the methods provided by N.C. Gen. Stat. ch. 15A, art. 53, he had waived his objections to that evidence. Because defendant's other exceptions were not supported by argument or authority they were deemed abandoned under N.C. R. App. P. 28(b)(5).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

 

154 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Blackwelder v. Winston-Salem


No. 518PA91


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


332 N.C. 319; 420 S.E.2d 432; 1992 N.C. LEXIS 487


April 16, 1992, Heard in the Supreme Court  
September 4, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On discretionary review pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-31, prior to determination by the Court of Appeals of an order granting partial summary judgment in favor of the plaintiff, entered by Seay, J., in Superior Court, Forsyth County on 27 August 1991.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant municipality sought review of an order entered in the Superior Court, Forsyth County (North Carolina) granting summary judgment against it in a tort action. Pursuant to its supervisory powers under N.C. Const. art. IV, § 12(1) the SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA treated the municipality's appeal as a petition for certiorari which it allowed.


OVERVIEW: A municipality challenged the lower court's granting of summary judgment against it in a tort action seeking damages based on alleged negligent leaf collecting. The court held that the municipality did not waive its governmental immunity from civil tort liability pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 160A-485(a) by organizing a corporation for the purpose of handling liability claims of $ 1,000,000 or less asserted against it. The court ruled that the municipality did not participate in a local government risk pool constituting a waiver of immunity under the statute because the municipality did not join with any other local government unit in the formation and operation of the corporation, and that the municipality did not purchase liability insurance by forming and operating the corporation because the municipality was bound to reimburse the corporation for any claims it paid for the municipality. The court also ruled that the municipality was not equitably estopped from raising the defense of governmental immunity because equitable estoppel could not be asserted against the municipality in its governmental capacity.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the summary judgment entered against the municipality in a tort claim filed against it, ruling that the municipality had not waived its governmental immunity by forming and operating a corporation for the purpose of handling liability claims against it.

 

155 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. Boyd


No. 547A88


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


332 N.C. 101; 418 S.E.2d 471; 1992 N.C. LEXIS 362


May 6, 1991, Heard in the Supreme Court  
July 17, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Currin, J., at the 17 October 1988 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Rockingham County, upon defendant's conviction by a jury of murder in the first degree.



DISPOSITION: New trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision from the Superior Court, Rockingham County (North Carolina), which convicted him of murder in the first degree and sentenced him to death. Defendant contended that the trial court erred in excusing a juror during the jury selection process and deferring her for service at a later session after a private, unrecorded bench conference and in denying defendant's motion for a state-paid mental health expert.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murdering his estranged wife and her father. Defendant was a Vietnam veteran, drank to excess, had numerous health problems, and had sought mental health counseling. Defendant contended that the trial court erred in excusing a juror during the jury selection process and deferring her for service at a later session after a private, unrecorded bench conference and in denying defendant's motion for a state-paid mental health expert to assist in his defense. On appeal, the court vacated the verdicts and judgments and remanded the case for a new trial. The court held that: (1) it was reversible error for the trial court to conduct a private conversation with the juror; (2) the substance of the conversation between the trial court and the juror was not revealed nor did the trial court reconstruct it at trial and thus the state could not demonstrate that the error was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt; and (3) the trial court erred in denying defendant's motion for a state-paid mental health expert on the ground that defendant was not represented by court-appointed counsel.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the trial court's judgments convicting defendant of murder and sentencing him to death. The court remanded the case for a new trial.

 

156 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. Price


No. 585A87


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


331 N.C. 620; 418 S.E.2d 169; 1992 N.C. LEXIS 405


April 10, 1991, Heard in the Supreme Court  
June 25, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Opinion on Remand of September 10, 1993, Reported at: 1993 N.C. LEXIS 393. Opinion on Remand of October 6, 1994, Reported at: 1994 N.C. LEXIS 579.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On remand from the Supreme Court of the United States. Additionally, on defendant's motion for appropriate relief filed in the Supreme Court on 30 April 1991, pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 15A-1418.

Original Opinion of February 7, 1990, Reported at: 1990 N.C. LEXIS 19. Opinion on Remand of October 3, 1990, Reported at: 1990 N.C. LEXIS 901.



DISPOSITION: Death sentence affirmed; mandate reinstated; case remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, after being convicted of first-degree murder, was sentenced to death. On appeal the court found no prejudicial error in either the guilt determination proceeding or the capital sentencing proceeding. The Supreme Court of the United States vacated the court's judgment and remanded the case for further consideration. The court heard the case on supplemental briefs ordered by the Supreme Court.


OVERVIEW: The Supreme Court directed that the court consider the question whether under McKoy there was error in the sentencing proceeding and, if so, whether the error was harmless. The court concluded that although there was McKoy error, the error was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt. Defendant also filed a motion for appropriate relief that assigned error to the trial court's failure to submit two mitigating circumstances to the jury. The court concluded that although the trial court erred in not submitting one statutory mitigating factor, that error was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt because none of the jurors would have found that circumstance to exist. The court held that the jury received the unanimity instruction found unconstitutional in McKoy. The court held that the state met its burden of showing that error was harmless because the trial court clerk's poll of the jury foreman and individual jury members demonstrated that the jury unanimously found only 1 of the 10 mitigating circumstances applied to defendant. The court held that the jury poll demonstrated unequivocally that the instruction did not prevent any juror's consideration of defendant's mitigating evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's death sentence, reinstated the mandate of its prior opinion, and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

 

157 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. Phipps


No. 565A90


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


331 N.C. 427; 418 S.E.2d 178; 1992 N.C. LEXIS 415


May 11, 1992, Heard in the Supreme Court  
June 25, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal of right by defendant pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of life imprisonment entered by Ross, J., at the 7 May 1990 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Wilkes County, upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of one count of first-degree murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to his conviction for robbery with a dangerous weapon was allowed by this Court on 25 February 1992.



DISPOSITION: First-degree murder (89 CRS 3312) -- no error. Robbery with a dangerous weapon (89 CRS 3311) -- judgment arrested.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a jury trial in the Superior Court, Wilkes County (North Carolina), which convicted defendant of first-degree murder and robbery with a dangerous weapon. The court granted defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals. Defendant contended that the trial court erred in failing to suppress certain inculpatory statements and in failing to instruct the jury on the lesser-included offense of second-degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's conviction stemmed from the discovery of the dead body of a restaurant bank manager in her car outside of a local bank, and the theft of two bank deposit bags that had been in the victim's possession. On appeal, the court held (1) the police did not conduct a custodial interrogation and defendant thus was not entitled to Miranda warnings prior to his subsequent confession to the murder, (2) defendant's confession was voluntarily made after being advised fully of his Miranda rights, (3) the trial court did not err in denying defendant's pretrial motions for authorization of a private investigator and an ex parte hearing at which to apply for funds to employ expert assistance, (4) there was no abuse of discretion in the admission of 52 color photographs of the crime scene and the victim's body, (5) the evidence was insufficient to warrant a jury instruction on voluntary intoxication, and (6) the trial court erred in denying defendant's request to submit to the jury the possible verdict of second-degree murder based on evidence that defendant may have killed the victim with malice but without premeditation and deliberation in the course of the robbery.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the trial court's conviction of defendant for first-degree murder under the felony murder rule. As a result of the trial court's error in refusing to instruct the jury on second-degree murder, the court arrested judgment on the conviction for the underlying felony, robbery with a dangerous weapon.

State v. Hill


No. 233A91


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


331 N.C. 387; 417 S.E.2d 765; 1992 N.C. LEXIS 421


February 12, 1992, Heard in the Supreme Court  
June 25, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment and sentence of death upon the defendant's conviction of first-degree murder, entered by Allen (C. Walter), J., in the Superior Court, Buncombe County, on 2 October 1990. The defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals on his appeal of an additional judgment for assault with a deadly weapon with intent to kill was allowed by the Supreme Court on 10 June 1991 and that appeal was consolidated with the defendant's appeal of the murder conviction.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court, Buncombe County (North Carolina), which convicted him of first-degree murder and assault with a deadly weapon with intent to kill.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder and assault with a deadly weapon with intent to kill and sentenced to death. Defendant sought review of the convictions and sentence. Pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000(e), there is an exclusive list of eleven aggravating circumstances which were to be considered by the jury in a capital case. The court held that defendant was properly apprised of all of the aggravating circumstances that the state could have relied upon in seeking the death penalty. The court held that evidence of defendant's other committed offenses was admissible under N.C. R. Evid. 404(b) because it was relevant to other facts at issue. The court held that advanced age could have been submitted for jury consideration as a mitigating circumstance upon proper evidence. The court determined that the trial court's failure to submit such nonstatutory mitigating circumstances on the jury form was harmless and free of prejudicial error. The court affirmed a judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment of the trial court, which convicted defendant of first-degree murder and assault with a deadly weapon with intent to kill. The court held that defendant was properly apprised of all of the aggravating circumstances that the state could have relied upon in seeking the death penalty through the statutory list. The trial court's failure to submit nonstatutory mitigating circumstances to the jury was harmless.

State v. Garner


No. 263A91


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


331 N.C. 491; 417 S.E.2d 502; 1992 N.C. LEXIS 422


February 14, 1992, Heard in the Supreme Court  
June 25, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right by defendant pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from judgment imposing a sentence of life imprisonment entered by Brewer, J., at the 15 October 1990 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Cumberland County, upon a jury verdict of guilty of first-degree murder on theories of both premeditation and deliberation and felony murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to his conviction of robbery with a dangerous weapon was allowed by this Court on 9 October 1991.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the Superior Court, Cumberland County (North Carolina), which convicted him of first-degree murder and robbery with a dangerous weapon, and assessed punishment at consecutive terms of life imprisonment for the murder charge and 25 years imprisonment for the armed robbery charge.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted in the lower court of first-degree murder and robbery with a dangerous weapon, and he appealed. The court allowed defendant's motion to bypass the appeals court with regard to his appeal of the armed robbery conviction and held that: 1) North Carolina adopted the inevitable discovery exception to the exclusionary rule; 2) in applying the inevitable discovery exception to the exclusionary rule, the question of whether the investigating officers acted in bad faith was irrelevant because the deterrence value of the exclusionary rule was inherently preserved by the risk of suppression faced by the state when it could not prove that evidence would have inevitably been discovered by legal, independent means, and the exclusion of physical evidence that would inevitably have been discovered added nothing to the fairness of the criminal trial; and 3) evidence that defendant attempted to murder a taxicab driver with the same handgun used to murder a convenience store clerk three weeks earlier was not more prejudicial than probative.


OUTCOME: The court found no error in defendant's conviction and sentences for first-degree murder and robbery with a dangerous weapon.

 

160 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Brannon v. North Carolina State Bd. of Elections


No. 102PA92


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


331 N.C. 335; 416 S.E.2d 390; 1992 N.C. LEXIS 271


April 16, 1992, Heard in the Supreme Court  
May 8, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On discretionary review prior to a determination by the Court of Appeals, pursuant to Rule 15(a) of the North Carolina Rules of Appellate Procedure and N.C.G.S. § 7A-31(b), of a judgment granting plaintiff's request for a mandatory injunction and writ of mandamus against defendants Board of Elections and Brock, by Herring, J., in the Superior Court, Wake County, on 28 February 1992.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina) entered a judgment that granted plaintiff judge's request for a mandatory injunction and writ of mandamus against defendants, North Carolina Board of Elections (Board) and individual members of the Board. Thereafter, defendants filed a direct appeal, and the Board and some other defendants petitioned for discretionary review prior to determination by the North Carolina Court of Appeals.


OVERVIEW: The Board declined to accept the judge's notice of candidacy for a seat on a state appellate court that was currently occupied. Thereafter, the judge filed an action for injunctive relief and mandamus against the Board and its individual members. The trial court entered judgment for the judge and issued a writ of mandamus ordering the Board to conduct an election. The Board had predicated its decision on the basis of eight-year judicial commissions that the governor had granted to certain appellate judges that had filled vacancies midway through the prior judge's term. On appeal, the court endeavored to determine if N.C. Gen. Stat. § 163-9, which provided that an election to a judicial vacancy permitted the elected judge to serve only for the unexpired term, violated N.C. Const. art. IV, § 16. The court in turn determined that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 163-9 was not prohibited by the North Carolina Constitution and was a lawful exercise of legislative power. Since the eight-year commissions issued by the governor were inconsistent with the statute, they were invalid. Thus, the judgment of the trial court was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court that ordered the Board to conduct elections for the relevant judicial seats was affirmed.

 

161 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. Hudson


No. 454A87


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


331 N.C. 122; 415 S.E.2d 732; 1992 N.C. LEXIS 211


February 12, 1992, Heard in the Supreme Court  
April 22, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a life sentence upon defendant's conviction of first-degree murder entered by Ross, J., at the 9 February 1987 Special Criminal Session of Superior Court, Guilford County. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals, pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-31, as to his second-degree murder conviction, for which he received a consecutive sentence of fifty years, was allowed by this Court 14 January 1992.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction for first degree murder, and sentence of life, in the Superior Court, Guilford County (North Carolina). Defendant claimed that the trial court committed reversible error in engaging in ex parte conversations that were not recorded and that took place out of the presence of defendant and his counsel.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that: (1) the trial court committed reversible error in engaging in ex parte conversations; (2) the trial court violated defendant's state constitutional right to be present at each stage of the capital proceeding; (3) conversations violated defendant's federal constitutional right to due process of law; (4) the jury was instructed improperly, and (5) his sentence was improperly calculated. The court was unimpressed by any contention. The court found that no reversible errors occurred at trial or sentencing. The court said there was no error when conversations between judge and jurors occurred because that could not have been considered to be a stage in the proceeding. The court said that the conversations were indisputably unintended and spontaneous, and not at the behest of the judge, occurring in the corridor of the courthouse during a trial recess. The court said by no stretch of the imagination could that have been construed as a stage in the proceeding. The court also disregarded the remainder of defendant's contentions, stating that no improper evidence or instructions were given at his trial, and that the sentencing was proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and sentence.

 

162 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Moore v. Knightdale Bd. of Elections


No. 480PA91


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


331 N.C. 1; 413 S.E.2d 541; 1992 N.C. LEXIS 142


February 10, 1992, Heard in the Supreme Court  
February 24, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On discretionary review prior to determination by the Court of Appeals, pursuant to Rule 15(a) of the North Carolina Rules of Appellate Procedure and N.C.G.S. § 7A-31(b), of a judgment entered on 12 August 1991 by Hight, J., in Superior Court, Wake County, permanently enjoining and restraining the enforcement of N.C.G.S. § 163-125 with respect to plaintiffs.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On discretionary review prior to determination by the appellate court, the court reviewed the judgment of the Superior Court of Wake County (North Carolina), which permanently enjoined and restrained the enforcement of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 163-125 (1991) by defendant election board with respect to plaintiffs, two mayoral candidates.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to the authority of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 163-125, the election board refused to allow two mayoral candidates to file for the office of mayor until they resigned their seats on the town council. The court held that § 163-125, the "resign to run" statute, was contrary to the express terms of N.C. Const. art. VI, § 6. The mayoral candidates were qualified voters in North Carolina, who were not disqualified from elective office by any provision of the state constitution. Thus, they were eligible for election by the people to office under the express language of N.C. Const. art. VI, § 6. The legislative attempt to require the resignation of those having the mayoral candidates' status as holders of another elective office imposed an additional qualification for elective office, not provided by the state constitution, and thus, failed to pass constitutional muster.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment below.

State v. Baldwin


No. 574A90


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


330 N.C. 446; 412 S.E.2d 31; 1992 N.C. LEXIS 5


November 15, 1991, Heard in the Supreme Court  
January 10, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of life imprisonment entered by Gaines, J., at the 23 July 1990 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Mecklenburg County, upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to his conviction of conspiracy to commit murder was allowed by this Court on 4 January 1991.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed an appeal as of right pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-27(a) challenging the judgment of the Criminal Session of Superior Court (North Carolina), which convicted defendant upon a jury verdict for first-degree murder and conspiracy to commit murder and imposed a sentence of life imprisonment. The court had granted defendant's motion to bypass the appellate court as to his conviction for conspiracy to commit murder.


OVERVIEW: A woman asked defendant to kill the victim for money. A co-conspirator took defendant to a second co-conspirator, who supplied defendant with a gun. Defendant went to the woman's apartment, where she instructed him to wait in a bedroom closet for the victim, who was shot when he opened the closet doors. Defendant challenged his conviction and sentencing for first-degree murder by premeditation and deliberation and murder perpetrated by lying in wait and for conspiracy to commit murder. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment and held that: (1) the denial of defendant's motion to prohibit the state from seeking the death penalty was proper because N.C. Const. art. I, § 24, did not require that mitigating circumstances be unanimously found by the jury; (2) defendant's hearsay statements to a psychologist was inadmissible because they were self-serving and exculpatory in relating to his defenses; (3) the expert's testimony as to the validity of defendant's confession was inadmissible because such issue required legal interpretations; and (4) a jury charge on voluntary intoxication was unwarranted because it was irrelevant to the first-degree murder by lying in wait charge.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court's judgment convicting defendant for first-degree murder and conspiracy to commit murder and sentencing defendant to life imprisonment.

Concord v. All Owners of Taxable Property etc.


No. 307PA91


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


330 N.C. 429; 410 S.E.2d 482; 1991 N.C. LEXIS 790


November 15, 1991, Heard in the Supreme Court  
December 6, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On discretionary review pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-31 prior to determination by the Court of Appeals of an order granting summary judgment in favor of plaintiff, entered by Davis (James C.), J., in Superior Court, Cabarrus County on 27 June 1991.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants owners of taxable property sought review of a decision from the Cabarrus County Superior Court (North Carolina), which granted a summary judgment in favor of plaintiff city in an action concerning the issuance of certain refunding bonds.


OVERVIEW: In an action concerning the issuance of certain obligation refunding bonds, the trial court granted a summary judgment in favor of the city. Owners of taxable property sought review, arguing that N.C. Const. art. V, § 4 authorized the issuance of refunding bonds without a vote of the people only for the purpose of refunding a valid existing debt and could not exceed the outstanding principal amount of the valid existing debt. On appeal, the court held that the Constitution did not so limit the General Assembly form authorizing municipalities to issue bonds and there was no requirement that the refunding indebtedness be less than or equal to the outstanding indebtedness. The court concluded that voter approval was not required when the purpose of the bonds issued was to refund a valid existing debt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of the city.

 

165 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Swanson v. State


No. 64PA91


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


330 N.C. 390; 410 S.E.2d 490; 1991 N.C. LEXIS 793



December 6, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Hearing on order granting plaintiffs' motion to rehear the opinion rendered in this case at 329 N.C. 576, 407 S.E.2d 791 (1991).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, two classes of taxpayers including federal retirees, filed a petition for rehearing of the court's opinion in the appeal by defendants, the state revenue department and associated individuals (state), of an order of the Superior Court, Wake County (North Carolina), which granted the taxpayers' motion for summary judgment and ordered a refund of taxes to both classes of taxpayers.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers sought a remand for a determination of their state law claims because they claimed that the superior court only granted them partial summary judgment. The court held that the order entered by the superior court from which the appeal was taken passed on all claims and was not for partial summary judgment because the order did not limit the order to claims under the United States Constitution. The court affirmed its previous opinion and reversed the superior court, holding that the granting of the tax exemptions for state retirees and national guard members did not violate N.C. Const. art. I, § 19 or N.C. Const. art. V, § 2 because the provision of pensions for retired state employees and the payment to members of the national guard were valid state objectives and were a legitimate governmental interest. The two classifications of taxpayers that received favorable treatment under the state income tax laws were reasonable and not arbitrary. The state taxes imposed on federal retiree taxpayers did not violate N.C. Const. art. I, § 16 because they were not taxes on acts previously done where the taxes were based on wages previously earned.


OUTCOME: The court granted rehearing and affirmed its previous opinion in favor of the state, reversing and remanding to the superior court for the entry of judgment dismissing the action.

State v. Canady


No. 278A90


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


330 N.C. 398; 410 S.E.2d 875; 1991 N.C. LEXIS 794


November 12, 1990, Heard in the Supreme Court  
December 6, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-30(2) from the decision of a divided panel of the Court of Appeals, 99 N.C. App. 189, 392 S.E.2d 457 (1990), finding no error in a judgment of Greene, J., at the 27 March 1989 session of Superior Court, Robeson County.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment by the Court of Appeals (North Carolina), which affirmed the trial court's finding of an aggravating factor based only on the prosecutor's statement in defendant's sentencing.


OVERVIEW: The trial court sentenced defendant after it found an aggravating factor based solely on the prosecutor's statement. The court of appeals affirmed the judgment, but on review, the court reversed. The court held that under the Fair Sentencing Act, the trial court could not find an aggravating factor where the only evidence to support it was the prosecutor's mere assertion that the factor existed. Moreover, the court found that defendant's silence at sentencing did not mean that he conceded to the accuracy of the prosecutor's statements. The court held that a separate objection to the statements was no longer required. The court held that a defendant did not have to object to each fact or factor at sentencing to preserve the issue on appeal. The court held that defendant's argument at sentencing that he be sentenced to the statutory minimum should have alerted the trial court to the fact that defendant did not want the trial court to find an aggravating factor.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's sentence and remanded the case because the trial court could not find an aggravating factor where the only evidence to support it was the prosecutor's mere assertion that the factor existed.

State v. Turner


No. 587A88


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


330 N.C. 249; 410 S.E.2d 847; 1991 N.C. LEXIS 796


September 11, 1991, Heard in the Supreme Court  
December 6, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal of right by defendant pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Battle, J., at the 12 September 1988 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Cumberland County, upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder.



DISPOSITION: Guilt phase: No error. Sentencing phase: New sentencing proceeding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Criminal Session of the Superior Court of Cumberland County (North Carolina), which convicted him of first-degree murder and sentenced him to death, arguing that he was entitled to a new capital sentencing proceeding.


OVERVIEW: The victim was seated in an automobile near a trailer when defendant walked up to the vehicle with a sawed-off shotgun, demanded payment of a debt from the victim, and then shot the victim in the head at close range. The victim died immediately. The defendant was convicted of first-degree murder based upon the theory of premeditation and deliberation. The jury recommended and the trial court imposed the death penalty. Although the court found that the guilt phase of defendant's trial was free from prejudicial error, the court concluded that he was entitled to a new capital sentencing proceeding. Defendant was entitled to a new capital sentencing proceeding because the trial court erroneously instructed the jury that it must be unanimous in order to find the existence of any mitigating circumstance that was offered by defendant. The instruction was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: Although the court found no prejudicial error in the guilt phase of defendant's capital trial for first-degree murder, the court granted defendant a new capital sentencing proceeding.

Martin v. State


No. 422PA91


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


330 N.C. 412; 410 S.E.2d 474; 1991 N.C. LEXIS 798; 57 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 1450


November 14, 1991, Heard in the Supreme Court  
December 6, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On discretionary review prior to a determination by the Court of Appeals, pursuant to Rule 15(a) of the North Carolina Rules of Appellate Procedure and N.C.G.S. § 7A-31(b), of a declaratory judgment and dismissal of plaintiffs' complaint by Jenkins, J., in Superior Court, Wake County, on 20 August 1991. Plaintiff Phillips appealed.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action which was styled as against defendant State of North Carolina, plaintiff judge sought review of a decision from the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina), which dismissed his complaint seeking a declaratory judgment of his rights under N.C. Const. art. IV, § 16, and N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-4.20, pertaining to his term of office and compulsory retirement.


OVERVIEW: The judge brought an action seeking a declaration of his rights under N.C. Const. art. IV, § 16, providing for an eight-year term of office and N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-4.20, requiring appellate judges to retire at 72. He contended that because he would reach 72 before the end of his respective eight-year terms, the statute unconstitutionally infringed upon his rights under the state constitution. The trial court dismissed the complaint. The judge appealed and petitioned the court for discretionary review prior to a determination of the intermediate court of appeals. The court granted the petition and held: (1) the words, "for service," as used in N.C. Const. art. IV, § 8, evinced an intent to empower the legislature to render particular judges ineligible for active service because of age notwithstanding that time might remain in their terms of office; and (2) where one provision of the state constitution could not violate another, it followed, that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7 A-4.20 did not violate other more general, substantive provisions, such as the equal protection provision, of the very constitution which, in another provision, expressly and specifically mandated its enactment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision from the trial court, which dismissed the judge's complaint.

Bailey v. State


No. 105PA91


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


330 N.C. 227; 412 S.E.2d 295; 1991 N.C. LEXIS 805; 14 Employee Benefits Cas. (BNA) 2156


May 8, 1991, Heard in the Supreme Court  
December 6, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected.
Writ of certiorari denied Bailey v. North Carolina, 504 U.S. 911, 118 L. Ed. 2d 547, 112 S. Ct. 1942, 1992 U.S. LEXIS 2737 (1992)


PRIOR HISTORY: On discretionary review prior to determination by the Court of Appeals pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-31 of an order of class certification entered on 31 December 1990 nunc pro tunc for 29 October 1990 and an order granting partial summary judgment for plaintiffs entered on 2 November 1990 by Lake, J., presiding at the 29 October 1990 session of Superior Court, Wake County.



DISPOSITION: Reversed in part and remanded; affirmed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant class sought review of a partial summary judgment in favor of respondent agents, in their individual capacities, by the superior court that upheld the validity of a repealed tax exemption under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-267 in favor of respondent state revenue agency. The court allowed the parties' joint petition for discretionary review prior to determination by the Court of Appeals (North Carolina).


OVERVIEW: Appellant class sought review of partial summary judgment upholding the validity of a repealed retirement plan tax exemption under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-267 in favor of respondent agents of respondent state revenue agency. The court found appellants' claim for a refund of taxes collected based on the repealed exemption should have been dismissed, as they failed to follow the statutory refund procedures. The court observed that a statutory provision was made for an action against the state, the procedure described by the statute had to be followed, and the remedies afforded were exclusive. The court further noted a constitutional defense to the validity of a tax did not exempt appellants from the mandatory procedure for challenging the tax. The court found in the present case all class demand letters were preceded by the filing of the complaint, and as a result, appellants failed to satisfy the condition precedent of demanding a refund before instituting an action against the revenue secretary. The court further found that absent specific statutory authorization for class or representative refund demands, such demands were ineffectual to satisfy the demand condition precedent.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part summary judgment for respondent agents, but reversed partial summary judgment in favor of appellant class, and remanded for entry of summary judgment in favor of respondent state revenue agency, for appellants' action challenging the validity of taxes collected under the repealed tax exemption was foreclosed by the failure to comply with statutory condition precedent to instituting such an action.

 

170 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Baker v. Martin


No. 246PA91


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


330 N.C. 331; 410 S.E.2d 887; 1991 N.C. LEXIS 806


September 9, 1991, Heard in the Supreme Court  
December 6, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On discretionary review pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-31 prior to determination by the Court of Appeals of an order granting summary judgment in favor of defendants, signed by Guice, J., out of court, session and county by consent of the parties and entered in the Superior Court, Watauga County on 9 May 1991.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff attorney sought review of a decision of the Superior Court, Watauga County (North Carolina), which granted summary judgment in favor of defendants, governor, attorney general, and bar president, in the attorney's action alleging that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-142 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The attorney was not considered for district judge because of his political party affiliation. He filed an action asserting that § 7A-142, which governed the appointment of persons to fill the unexpired terms of district court judges, violated the Constitution of North Carolina by placing the additional qualification of candidates that they were to be the same political party as the vacating judge. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of the governor, attorney general, and bar president. On appeal, the court affirmed. Unless the constitution expressly or by necessary implication restricted the actions of the legislative branch, the General Assembly was free to implement legislation as long as that legislation did not offend some specific constitutional provision. N.C. Const. art. VI, § 6 did expressly limit disqualifications to office for those who were elected by the people to those disqualifications set out in the constitution. However, no provision of the constitution so limited disqualifications to office for those who were appointed, whether the appointment were for a purely appointive office or to fill the unexpired term of an elective office.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, which granted summary judgment in favor of the governor, attorney general, and bar president, in the attorney's action alleging that the statute which governed the appointment of person to fill the unexpired terms of district court judges violated the Constitution of North Carolina.

State v. Case

DISPOSITION: New trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction in the Superior Court, Gaston County (North Carolina) for first degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder and sentenced to death. On appeal, defendant argued that the court should set aside his guilty plea. Upon review, the court ordered a new trial to protect the constitutionality of its capital sentencing system. The court found that the State erred when it agreed to present evidence of only one aggravating circumstance if defendant agreed to plead guilty to first degree murder. The court noted that the State did not have the discretion to decide whether a first degree murder action should be tried as a capital case.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction and ordered a new trial.

 

172 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. Greene


No. 456A87


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


329 N.C. 771; 408 S.E.2d 185; 1991 N.C. LEXIS 605


February 14, 1991, Heard in the Supreme Court  
September 5, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On remand from the Supreme Court of the United States.



DISPOSITION: Death sentence vacated; remanded for new capital sentencing proceeding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Supreme Court of the United States vacated the judgment of the SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA and ordered the court to determine whether a capital sentence was proper. The court denied defendant's motion to remand the case to the superior court for the imposition of a sentence of life imprisonment, and the court heard arguments on whether there was an error in defendant's sentencing proceeding and whether the error was harmless.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of the first degree murder of his father and sentenced to death. Defendant challenged his sentence. The Supreme Court of the United States vacated the court's judgment and remanded the case to the court for further consideration. Defendant claimed that the jury was not instructed that they were permitted to take into account any mitigating circumstances under the McKoy standard. After a hearing, the court agreed, vacated the sentence, and remanded the case to the superior court for a new sentencing hearing. The court held that (1) even though the same evidence supporting the mental or emotional disturbance circumstances were considered by the jury on the rejected impaired capacity circumstance, the McKoy error was not rendered harmless; (2) it would not have been inconsistent for one or more jurors, considering the same evidence in support of both circumstances, to find that defendant was mentally or emotionally disturbed but that his capacity to appreciate his conduct's criminality or to conform his conduct to law was not impaired; and (3) the trial court did not err in failing to permit the introduction of voluntary intoxication evidence to the jury.


OUTCOME: On remand, the court vacated the death sentence of defendant and remanded the case to the superior court for a new hearing because there was error in defendant's initial sentencing hearing.

Swanson v. State


No. 64PA91


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


329 N.C. 576; 407 S.E.2d 791; 1991 N.C. LEXIS 518; 60 U.S.L.W. 2172; 14 Employee Benefits Cas. (BNA) 1213


May 7, 1991, Heard in the Supreme Court  
August 14, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Granted December 6, 1991, Reported at 1991 N.C. LEXIS 793.


PRIOR HISTORY: On discretionary review pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-31 prior to determination by the Court of Appeals of orders entered by Currin, J., on 17 August 1990, 3 October 1990 and 31 December 1990 in Superior Court, Wake County.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a class action, the Superior Court, Wake County (North Carolina) rendered judgment for plaintiff taxpayers holding defendant, state and its officers and agencies, liable for refunds for taxes paid on federal pensions or military pay before a precedent was decided. The state and its officers and agencies appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the precedent did not apply retroactively because (1) it was a case of first impression; (2) the decision was not clearly foreshadowed as evidenced by the fact that 23 states had plans similar to the exemption of state employees from taxation of their pensions, which had been in effect since 1941 without court challenge; (3) retroactive application would have no effect on the operation of the principle that the precedent would advance because the general assembly repealed the provisions of the tax system at which the precedent was aimed; (4) the state acted reasonably under the law as it was then understood when it exempted the benefits of state pensioners from income tax and it excluded part of the pay of National Guardsmen and it would be inequitable to require the state to refund the taxes paid by federal pensioners and military personnel in light of the state's dire financial straits and the cost of refunds. The rule the court applied to decide whether the precedent was retroactive was invoked by pleading no retroactive relief. The state did not waive its right to refuse a refund of these taxes. The taxpayers were not entitled to refunds.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment holding the state and its officers and agencies liable for refunds for taxes paid on federal pensions or military pay. The court remanded for the entry of a judgment dismissing the taxpayers' action.

 

174 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. Wynne


No. 541A88


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


329 N.C. 507; 406 S.E.2d 812; 1991 N.C. LEXIS 527


April 8, 1991, Heard in the Supreme Court  
August 14, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right by defendant pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment sentencing him to death for conviction of murder in the first degree, entered by Allsbrook, J., at the 24 October 1988 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Halifax County.



DISPOSITION: Guilt phase: No error. Penalty phase: Death sentence vacated and case remanded for new capital sentencing proceeding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was found guilty by a jury at the Criminal Session of Superior Court, Halifax County (North Carolina) of murder in the first degree and kidnapping and sentenced to death. Defendant appealed the conviction and the sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant confessed that he beat the victim, drove him to a field, and stabbed him in the chest. The court rejected defendant's juror selection challenges. One juror was properly excused for views on the death penalty, one was too emotional, and one worked with defendant's mother. Evidence of defendant's racism was admissible to prove motive. Photographs of the victim's maggot-infested, decomposing body were not used excessively or solely to arouse the jury's passions. Defendant's confession established a conspiracy, so statements by his co-conspirator were admissible against defendant even though conspiracy was not charged. Defendant's statement to officers that he did not care if he received capital punishment was relevant to his state of mind when he confessed. Admission of hearsay evidence from two officers about statements made by defendant and his co-conspirator was harmless error; the statements were corroborated by other witnesses. In the penalty phase, it was reversible error to instruct the jurors that they had to unanimously find there was evidence of defendant's impaired capacity before they could consider that mitigating factor in making a sentencing decision.


OUTCOME: The court found no error in the guilt phase; it vacated the sentence of death and remanded for a new capital sentencing proceeding.

State v. Artis


No. 504A84


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


329 N.C. 679; 406 S.E.2d 827; 1991 N.C. LEXIS 526


March 12, 1991, Heard in the Supreme Court  
August 14, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of mandamus denied State v. Artis, 2010 N.C. LEXIS 270 (N.C., Mar. 11, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On remand from the Supreme Court of the United States.
Artis v. North Carolina, 494 U.S. 1023, 110 S. Ct. 1466, 108 L. Ed. 2d 604, 1990 U.S. LEXIS 1467 (1990)



DISPOSITION: Death sentence vacated; remanded for a new capital sentencing proceeding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On remand from the Supreme Court of the United States, the court was directed to reevaluate defendant's capital sentencing proceeding.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of rape and first-degree murder. Defendant was sentenced to death. On appeal, the court found no error in either the guilt proceeding or the capital sentencing proceeding against defendant. The United States Supreme Court granted defendant's petition for writ of certiorari, vacated the court's judgment and remanded the case for further consideration. On remand, the court denied defendant's motion for the imposition of a life sentence, but ordered supplemental briefs limited to the question of whether there was error in defendant's capital sentencing proceedings. The United States Supreme Court had found that it was unconstitutional under the Eighth and Fourteenth Amendments jury instructions in capital proceedings that required juries to be unanimous in the finding of mitigating circumstances. An error under the Supreme Court's recent ruling could not be held to be harmless if there was substantial evidence to support two or more of the mitigating circumstances. The court found that the jury in defendant's trial was erroneously instructed based upon the Supreme Court's recent ruling and remanded the case for a new capital proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court held that the jury in defendant's trial was erroneously instructed and remanded the case for a new capital sentencing proceeding.

 

176 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. Lloyd


No. 577A85


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


329 N.C. 662; 407 S.E.2d 218; 1991 N.C. LEXIS 528


March 15, 1991, Heard in the Supreme Court  
August 14, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On remand from the Supreme Court of the United States.
State v. Lloyd, 327 N.C. 477, 397 S.E.2d 230, 1990 N.C. LEXIS 907 (1990)



DISPOSITION: Death sentence vacated; remanded for new capital sentencing proceeding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On remand from defendant's second petition for a writ of certiorari to the United States Supreme Court, which vacated and remanded the court's second judgment, the court considered defendant's appeal of his conviction for first-degree murder and his death sentence. Defendant argued that there was reversible error in the sentencing proceeding because the court required unanimous findings of the existence of mitigating circumstances.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder and was sentenced to death. On appeal to the United States Supreme Court, no error was found, but the judgment was later vacated, and the case was remanded to the court, which reinstated its earlier mandate. On defendant's second petition for writ of certiorari, the United States Supreme Court vacated the court's second judgment and remanded for further consideration. The court vacated the sentence and determined that there was reversible error entitling defendant to a new capital sentencing proceeding. The state did not meet its burden of proving that the capital sentencing jury instructions were constitutional. The state conceded that the jury instructions required the jury to find unanimously the existence of a mitigating circumstance before individual jurors could consider that circumstance or evidence supporting it in the final sentencing decision. Such instructions were prejudicial error that violated the Eighth and Fourteenth Amendments. Evidence showed that reasonable jurors could have found that defendant had no significant history of criminal activity, a mitigating circumstance under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-2000 (1988).


OUTCOME: Upon second remand, the court vacated defendant's death sentence and remanded the case for a new capital sentencing proceeding due to prejudicial error in the jury instructions that required unanimity in the finding of a statutory mitigating circumstance.

State v. Crawford


No. 443A89


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


329 N.C. 466; 406 S.E.2d 579; 1991 N.C. LEXIS 531


March 13, 1991, Heard in the Supreme Court  
August 14, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of life imprisonment entered by Hight, J., at the 24 April 1989 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Alamance County, upon a jury verdict of guilty of first-degree murder.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment by the Superior Court, Alamance County (North Carolina), which convicted him of first-degree murder of his girlfriend's son whose death was caused by water intoxication. Defendant contended that there was insufficient evidence presented of premeditation and deliberation and of murder by torture and that the trial court's instructions on murder by torture were inadequate.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed, finding the record showed defendant had developed a pattern of using extraordinary methods to punish and humiliate the boy and the boy's death was a result of a disciplinary method. The court found testimony by a witness qualified as an expert in pediatric critical care medicine was admissible under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 8C-1, N.C. R. Evid. 702(1988). The court held the qualification of an expert witness was left to the sound discretion of the trial judge. The expert's testimony as to the ultimate issue under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 8C-1, N.C. R. Evid. 704 (1988) was not objectionable and the testimony that utilized several legal terms and concepts was proper. The court held defendant admitted he coerced the boy to drink large quantities of water, which ultimately caused the death. The court held that malice was not a prerequisite of murder by torture under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-17, which was classified as first-degree murder as there was no reference to malice. Since murder was not defined by statute, the trial court employed the common law definition under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 4-1(1986) and neither premeditation nor deliberation was an element of the crime.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of first-degree murder.

State v. Arnold


No. 245A90


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


329 N.C. 128; 404 S.E.2d 822; 1991 N.C. LEXIS 405


March 14, 1991, Heard in the Supreme Court  
June 12, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by the State pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-30(2) from a decision of a divided panel of the Court of Appeals, 98 N.C. App. 518, 392 S.E.2d 140 (1990), reversing in part the judgment of imprisonment entered by Stevens, J., on 15 February 1988 in Superior Court, Sampson County. Defendant petitioned for, and this Court allowed, discretionary review of additional issues.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State of North Carolina appealed, pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-30(2), from a decision of the Court of Appeals (North Carolina), which reversed in part the judgment of imprisonment against defendant in a case involving murder in the first degree and conspiracy to commit murder in the first degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted for murder in the first degree and conspiracy to commit murder in the first degree as a result of the stabbing death of her husband. She was found guilty of conspiracy and murder in the second degree. The State appealed from the appellate court's holding that the trial court had erred by submitting as a possible verdict murder in the second degree. The appellate court affirmed the conspiracy conviction. The court affirmed the judgment below. It held that the trial court erred in submitting the possible verdict of murder in the second degree, as the evidence supported only a possible verdict of murder in the first degree. The court concluded that the evidence against defendant was not overwhelming because it could have raised doubts in the minds of reasonable jurors. The court noted that, had not the inviting verdict of murder in the second degree been available to the jury, the verdict may well have been one of not guilty. Because the State failed to prove that the error was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt, the court held that defendant was prejudiced by the trial court's error and that her conviction for murder in the second degree was properly reversed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's reversal of defendant's conviction for murder in the second degree due to the trial court's error in submitting murder in the second degree as a possible verdict. The judgment upholding defendant's conviction for conspiracy was affirmed.

 

179 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. Quick


No. 541A87


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


329 N.C. 1; 405 S.E.2d 179; 1991 N.C. LEXIS 408


December 10, 1990, Heard in the Supreme Court  
June 12, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal of right by defendant pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Washington, J., at the 24 August 1987 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Richmond County, upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder.



DISPOSITION: Guilt phase: no error. Sentencing phase: new sentencing proceeding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder on the basis of premeditation and deliberation and felony murder. He was also convicted of robbery with a dangerous weapon. The jury recommended that defendant be sentenced to death for the first degree murder. The Superior Court, Richmond County (North Carolina), imposed a sentence of death for the murder and arrested judgment on the robbery charge. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court found that no prejudicial errors had occurred during the guilt phase of the trial. However, the court did find that the trial court had erroneously instructed the jury regarding its deliberations on the mitigating factors presented by the defendant. The trial court instructed the jury that it could not consider any circumstance in mitigation of the offense unless the jury unanimously concluded that the circumstance had been proved. The trial court then instructed the jury to answer "no" to each mitigating circumstance that it failed to find unanimously. The court determined that, under the circumstances, the error was not harmless because the court could not conclude beyond a reasonable doubt that the erroneous unanimity instruction did not preclude one or more jurors from considering, as mitigation, defendant's good work record or his diminished capacity to appreciate the criminality of his act or to conform his conduct to the requirements of the law. Nor could the court conclude that the jury, acting under proper instructions, would nevertheless have imposed the death penalty. Accordingly, defendant was entitled to a new sentencing proceeding.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was affirmed. The judgment of sentence was reversed and the case was remanded for resentencing.

State v. Joyner


No. 226A89


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


329 N.C. 211; 404 S.E.2d 653; 1991 N.C. LEXIS 418


May 7, 1991, Heard in the Supreme Court  
June 12, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal of right by defendant pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Winberry, J., at the 15 May 1989 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Nash County, upon a jury verdict finding defendant guilty of first-degree murder. On 17 May 1990 this Court allowed defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals on his related robbery with a dangerous weapon and secret assault charges.



DISPOSITION: Case No. 88CRS15500, first-degree murder: Guilt phase, no error; sentencing phase, new sentencing proceeding. Case No. 88CRS15501, robbery with a dangerous weapon: No error. Case No. 88CRS15502, secret assault: Judgment arrested.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the Superior Court, Nash County (North Carolina), which convicted him of first-degree murder, robbery with a dangerous weapon, and secret assault.


OVERVIEW: Defendant confessed to the robbery and murder of the victim, and was convicted of first-degree murder, robbery with a dangerous weapon, and secret assault. The murder conviction was based on premeditation and deliberation, felony murder, and lying in wait, and defendant was sentenced to death. On appeal, defendant alleged that there was insufficient evidence of premeditation for the submission of the first-degree murder charge to the jury. The court disagreed, holding that defendant's confession established that his shooting of the victim had been totally unprovoked and was premeditated and deliberate. The court held that because the jury was erroneously and prejudicially instructed on a unanimity requirement for finding mitigating circumstances, defendant was entitled to a new sentencing procedure. Finally, the court held that because the murder conviction was based on defendant's lying in wait, the secret assault conviction was improper, so the court arrested the judgment entered upon the secret assault conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed an order from the trial court, which convicted defendant of first-degree murder and robbery with a dangerous weapon. The court arrested the judgment convicting defendant of secret assault and a new sentencing phase was ordered on the murder conviction.

State v. Vance


No. 202PA90 - Forsyth


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


328 N.C. 613; 403 S.E.2d 495; 1991 N.C. LEXIS 324


February 14, 1991, Heard in the Supreme Court  
May 2, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected May 8, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: On discretionary review, pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-31, of the decision of the Court of Appeals, 98 N.C. App. 105, 390 S.E.2d 165 (1990), which found no error in the trial of the defendant or in the judgment for second degree murder entered on 2 February 1988 by Freeman, J., in the Superior Court, Forsyth County.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeals (North Carolina), which affirmed his conviction for second degree murder and the denial of his motion to dismiss the charges. Defendant contended, inter alia, that the murder charge against him should have been dismissed under the common law "year and a day" rule because uncontroverted evidence showed that the victim had died 14 months after the collision.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was driving under the influence when his car struck a couple's car. The wife died immediately and the husband died 14 months later. Defendant was convicted of second-degree murder in the husband's death. On appeal, he contended that the trial court should have granted his motion to dismiss the charges based on the common law "year and a day" rule. The court reversed and held that the year and a day rule was obsolete within the meaning of that term as used in N.C. Gen. Stat. § 4-1, and was no longer part of the common law of North Carolina for any purpose. The court concluded that because of the prohibitions against ex post facto laws embodied in U.S. Const. amends. V and XIV, its decision abolishing the year and a day rule had prospective effect only. Thus, defendant's conviction for second-degree murder was in error. However, the court further ruled that, in finding defendant guilty of second-degree murder, the jury necessarily had to find facts establishing the lesser-included offense of involuntary manslaughter, and it remanded for a judgment of guilt of involuntary manslaughter.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction and remanded with instructions for a judgment of guilty of a lesser-included offense.

 

182 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. Roper


No. 301A88 - Burke


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


328 N.C. 337; 402 S.E.2d 600; 1991 N.C. LEXIS 251


October 9, 1990, Heard in the Supreme Court  
April 3, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected May 13, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal as of right pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-27(a) from a judgment imposing a sentence of death entered by Lamm, J., at the 17 June 1988 Criminal Session of Superior Court, Burke County. Defendant's motion to bypass the Court of Appeals as to his convictions of first-degree rape and first-degree kidnapping as a habitual felon was allowed by this Court on 21 October 1988.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Burke County (North Carolina), which convicted him in a jury trial of first-degree murder, first-degree rape, and first-degree kidnapping as a habitual felon and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the defendant's conviction and death sentence, finding that he had received a fair trial and a sentencing proceeding free of prejudicial error. The court held that the trial court neither abused its discretion nor violated the defendant's constitutional rights in denying his motion for a continuance to locate a witness to testify on his defense of self-defense. The court found that the State was not culpable because the government had made extensive efforts to locate the witness after serving him with a subpoena. The evidence indicated that the witness had gone into hiding and was unlikely to appear in a reasonable time. The court found that the defendant had the benefit of a substantial portion of the witness's pretrial statements made to the sheriff's department and to his counsel. The court held that a deputy sheriff's testimony as to what the murder victim told him on the day he died was admissible under the residual exception to the hearsay rule. The court reasoned that the victim was in fear of his life, that he was proceeding to the magistrate's office when he was killed, and his unavailability was a result of his death at the hands of the defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in convicting the defendant of first-degree murder, first-degree rape, and first-degree kidnapping as a habitual felon and in sentencing him to death.

 

183 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


City of New Bern v. New Bern-Craven County Bd. of Educ.


No. 157PA89 - Craven


SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA


328 N.C. 557; 402 S.E.2d 623; 1991 N.C. LEXIS 259


November 16, 1989, Heard in the Supreme Court  
April 3, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On discretionary review pursuant to N.C.G.S. § 7A-31 prior to a determination by the Court of Appeals of a judgment for defendants entered by Reid, J., at the 28 December 1988 Session of Superior Court in Craven County.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged a judgment of the Court of Appeals (North Carolina), which affirmed a judgment of the trial court in favor of respondents, school board, trustees, college, medical authority, county, and attorney general, holding that the city did not have a cognizable action under the Declaratory Judgment Act, N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 1-253 et seq., to provide county administration of building and safety codes inside the city limits.


OVERVIEW: The city contended that the trial court did not maintain jurisdiction under the Declaratory Judgment Act, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-253 et seq., to determine the validity of laws adopted by the General Assembly to provide that the county shall administer building and safety codes inside the city limits. The court reversed the trial court's judgment, which was affirmed by the court of appeals, and held that the trial court did not maintain jurisdiction. The county contended it had the right to enforce certain laws. The city alleged that the county did not have the right. The court held that a controversy existed and that it was a justiciable controversy. Thus, it could have been determined by a declaratory judgment action. The city's status was changed by the acts of the general assembly. The city was entitled to challenge this change of status by an action for a declaratory judgment. Whether there was an actual controversy did not depend on the existence of a vested right. The city had the right to enforce the codes prior to the action by the general assembly. The change in status could be determined under the Declaratory Judgment Act.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of the school board, trustees, college, medical authority, county, and attorney general, which held that the city did not have a cognizable action under the Declaratory Judgment Act.
